Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
12 min
Rulings on Acts of Persons with Restricted Capacity
Rulings on acts of children and the insane

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the Hanafi ruling for any act performed by a person with restricted capacity by categorizing the act as pure good, pure evil, or intermediate, and apply the corresponding rule regarding validity and liability.

Core Idea
For restricted capacity, pure good/evil acts are fully valid, but intermediate acts are valid only as supererogatory and without liability.

Now that we know who has restricted capacity, we must understand how the Sharia evaluates their actions. Imam al-Bazdawi divides acts into three categories: pure good (faith), pure evil (disbelief), and intermediate (prayer, contracts, etc.). Each category has a distinct ruling regarding validity and liability. Let's work through these rulings with examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Categorize the act as pure good, pure evil, or intermediate
Read the passage: 'فأما حقوق الله تعالى، فمنها ما هو حسن لا يحتمل غيره... ومنها ما هو قبيح لا يحتمل غيره... ومن ذلك ما هو بين هذين القسمين.' Identify which category applies based on the nature of the act. Faith is pure good, disbelief is pure evil, and all other acts (prayer, fasting, transactions) are intermediate.
Pure good and pure evil are characterized by admitting no alternative and having an intrinsic moral quality independent of legal obligation.
2
Apply ruling for pure good acts
For faith (pure good), the passage says: 'فوجب القول بصحته من الصبي' – it is necessary to rule its validity from a child. There is no liability (عهدة) because it is good and admits no alternative. The act occurs in reality and is not prohibited by Sharia.
The validity of pure good acts is based on the principle that something occurring in reality is not annulled without a Sharia prohibition.
3
Apply ruling for pure evil acts
For disbelief (pure evil), the passage rules that it is not forgiven; it is valid (i.e., it has consequences) in the hereafter and in some worldly matters (e.g., inheritance, marriage). It is treated as a real act despite restricted capacity.
Pure evil acts are considered real because they are the opposite of pure good and their harm cannot be dismissed.
4
Apply ruling for intermediate acts
For intermediate acts, the passage says: 'فقلنا فيه بصحة الأداء من غير عهدة' – validity of execution without liability. They are valid as supererogatory acts (تطوعاً), no binding performance, no obligation of makeup. Example: child's ihram – valid, but if he commits a prohibited act or is prevented, nothing is due.
This ruling reflects that for restricted capacity, the Sharia does not impose obligations or penalties for intermediate acts; they are purely voluntary.
5
Review the example of ihram to confirm the rule
The passage gives a concrete example: a child assumes ihram, then is prevented (ihsar) – no makeup. If he commits a prohibition (like hunting), nothing is due. This illustrates the absence of liability (عهدة) for intermediate acts.
Note the analogy: an adult who mistakenly thinks an act is obligatory has the binding lifted; similarly, a child's act is free from binding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب
أهلية الأداء
وأما أهلية الأداء فنوعان : قاصر، وكامل.
أما القاصر فيثبت بقدرة البدن إذا كانت قاصرةً قبل البلوغ، وكذلك بعد البلوغ فيمن كان معتوهاً؛ لأنه بمنزلة الصبي؛ لأنه عاقل لم يعتدل عقله.
وأصل العقل يُعرف بدلالة العيان، وذلك أن يختار المرء ما يصلح له بدَرْك العواقب المستورة فيما يأتيه ويَذَرُه، وكذلك القصور يُعرف بالامتحان.
فأما الاعتدال : فأمر يتفاوت فيه البشر ، فإذا ترقى عن رتبة القصور: أُقيم البلوغ مقامَ الاعتدال في أحكام الشرع، والأحكام في هذا الباب منقسمة على ما مر.
فأما حقوق الله تعالى، فمنها ما هو حسن لا يحتمل غيره، ولا عهدةَ فيه بوجه، وهو الإيمان بالله تعالى، فوجب القول بصحته من الصبي لما ثبتت أهلية أدائه ، ووجد منه بحقيقته ؛ لأن الشيء إذا وجد بحقيقته : لم ينعدم إلا بحَجْرٍ من الشرع، وذلك في الإيمان باطل؛ لما قلنا إنه حسن لا يحتمل غيره، ولا عهداً فيه إلا في لزوم أدائه، وذلك يحتمل الوضع عنه، فأما الأداء فخال عن العهدة ؛ لأن حرمان الإرث يضاف إلى كفر الباقي، وكذلك الفرقة، ولأن ما يلزمه بعد الإيمان فمن ثمراته. وإنما تتعرف صحة الشيء من حكمه الذي وضع له، وهو سعادة الآخرة، لا من ثمراته ألا يُرى أنها تلزمه إذا ثبت له حكم الإيمان تبعاً لغيره، ولم يُعد عهدة.
ومنها ما هو قبيح لا يحتمل غيره، وهو الجهل بالصانع، والكفر به، ألا يُرى أنه لا يُرَدُّ علمه بوالديه فكيف يُرَدُّ علمه بالله تعالى؟! وكذلك الجهل بغير الله تعالى لا يُعدُّ منه علماً، فكيف الجهل بالله تعالى؟!
وإذا كان كذلك : لم يصلح أن تُجعل ردَّتُه عفواً، بل كان صحيحاً في أحكام الآخرة، وما يلزمه من أحكام الدنيا بالردة: فإنما يلزمه حكماً لصحته، لا قصداً إليه، فلم يصلح العفو عن مثله ، كما إذا ثبت تبعاً.
ومن ذلك : ما هو بين هذين القسمين فقلنا فيه بصحة الأداء من غير عهدة، حتى قلنا بسقوط الوجوب في الكل ؛ لأن اللزوم لا يخلو عن العهدة، وقد شرعت بدون ذلك الوصف، وقلنا بصحتها تطوعاً بلا لزوم مضي، ولا وجوب قضاء؛ لأنها قد شرعت كذلك.
ألا يُرى أن البالغ إذا شَرع فيها على ظنّ أنها عليه وليست عليه: أن اللزوم يبطل عنه.
وكذلك إذا شرع في الإحرام على هذا الوجه، ثم أُحصر: فلا قضاء عليه، فقلنا في الصبي إذا أحرم صح منه بلا عهدة، حتى إذا ارتكب محظوراً لم يلزمه شيء.
Chapter of Capacity for Execution. As for capacity for execution, it is of two types: restricted and complete. As for restricted, it is established by bodily ability when it is deficient before puberty, and similarly after puberty for one who is insane because he is like a child; he is rational but his reason is not balanced. The foundation of reason is known by direct evidence, namely that a person chooses what is beneficial for him by perceiving hidden consequences in what he does and refrains from. Similarly, deficiency is known by examination. As for balance, it is a matter that varies among humans. When it rises above the level of deficiency, puberty is made the equivalent of balance in the rulings of Sharia. The rulings in this chapter are divided as mentioned. As for the rights of Allah Almighty, among them is what is good and admits no alternative, and there is no liability in it at all, and that is faith in Allah Almighty. So it is necessary to rule its validity from a child because his capacity for execution is established and it occurred from him in its reality. For if something occurs in its reality, it is not annulled except by a prohibition from the Sharia. That is invalid in faith because we said it is good and admits no alternative, and there is no covenant except in the obligation of its performance, and that can be waived; as for performance, it is free from liability. Because deprivation of inheritance is attributed to the disbelief of the remaining [heirs], and similarly separation, and because what becomes obligatory after faith is from its fruits. The validity of a thing is known by its ruling for which it was legislated, which is happiness in the hereafter, not from its fruits. Do you not see that it becomes binding on him when the ruling of faith is established for him as a consequence of others, and liability is not counted? Among them is what is evil and admits no alternative, which is ignorance of the Creator and disbelief in Him. Do you not see that his knowledge of his parents is not rejected, so how can his knowledge of Allah Almighty be rejected? Similarly, ignorance of other than Allah Almighty is not considered knowledge from him, so how about ignorance of Allah Almighty? Since this is so, it is not proper to make his apostasy forgiven; rather, it is valid in the rulings of the hereafter. And what becomes binding on him from the worldly rulings due to apostasy, it becomes binding as a ruling due to its validity, not as an intention towards it. So forgiveness for such is not proper, just as when it is established as a consequence. Among that is what is between these two categories. So we said regarding it the validity of execution without liability, to the extent that we said the obligation drops completely because binding does not lack liability, and it was legislated without that description. We said its validity as a supererogatory act without binding performance and without obligation of makeup, because it was legislated thus. Do you not see that if an adult begins [an act] thinking it is obligatory upon him while it is not, the binding is nullified for him. Similarly, if he begins ihram in this manner, then is prevented, there is no makeup upon him. So we said regarding a child, if he assumes ihram, it is valid from him without liability, such that if he commits a prohibited act, nothing is incumbent upon him.
Source pages p. 716

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Imam al-Bazdawi on capacity for execution (أهلية الأداء). It begins by defining two types: restricted and complete. Restricted capacity pertains to those before puberty and the insane, whose bodily ability is present but reasoning is incomplete. The author then explains that the validity of acts from such persons depends on the nature of the act itself. Acts that are purely good (like faith) are valid because they admit no alternative and bring no liability. Acts that are purely evil (like disbelief) are also considered valid and entail consequences because they cannot be ignored. Acts that fall in between (like prayer or fasting) are valid from a child but without binding obligation or liability; he may perform them as supererogatory. The example of ihram illustrates: a child's ihram is valid, but if he violates a prohibition, no penalty is due because there is no liability. This nuanced approach reflects the Hanafi understanding that capacity is established for these categories based on the intrinsic nature of the act, not merely on the person's legal status.

Key Terms
تطوع (taṭawwuʿ): Supererogatory act; an act of worship performed voluntarily without being obligatory.
إحرام (iḥrām): The state of consecration for Hajj or Umrah, involving specific prohibitions.
قضاء (qaḍāʾ): Making up a missed obligatory act; compensatory performance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for a child's act that falls between pure good and pure evil (e.g., prayer)?
A) It is invalid until puberty
B) It is valid and binding with obligation of makeup if missed
C) It is valid as a supererogatory act without liability
D) It is valid only if the child intends it as obligatory

Key Takeaway
Persons with restricted capacity have valid acts, but intermediate acts carry no liability or obligation, while pure good and evil acts have full validity with consequences.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Ruling on Pure Harm Actions (Darar Mahd) for Minors and Slaves
States the rule on pure harm actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an action as pure harm (darar mahd) and state its invalidity when performed by a minor or slave, and explain the exception for a loan ordered by a judge.

Core Idea
Pure harm actions by a minor or slave are void; only a judge may compel a loan to preserve rights.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the legal capacity of a minor or slave is restricted. This passage introduces the concept of 'darar mahd' (pure harm) — actions that offer no benefit to the agent. Such actions are not valid from a minor or slave. However, there is a nuance: a loan, though normally pure harm, can become beneficial under judicial authority, allowing the judge to compel it. This lesson dissects this rule.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of 'darar mahd'
Identify what makes an action pure harm: it must cause loss with no countervailing benefit (e.g., financial loss or obligation).
Examples: divorce imposes maintenance; freeing a slave loses an asset.
2
State the rule for minors/slaves
Such actions are 'not legislated' (ghayr mashru') as valid from them; direct performance is void (batil).
The agent lacks the legal capacity (ahliyyat ada') to perform harmful acts.
3
Memorize the listed examples
Divorce (talaq), freeing slaves (itaq), gift (hiba), loan (qard), charity (sadaqa).
These are all acts that deplete wealth or create liabilities.
4
Identify the exception
A judge (qadi) may compel a loan because under judicial authority it transforms into pure benefit (e.g., to pay urgent debts).
The loan becomes beneficial because it preserves rights, shifting its category.
5
Analyze the reasoning
The judge's custody over rights (wilayat al-qada) changes the nature of the act: the benefit of preserving rights outweighs the loss of giving the loan.
This shows how a 'pure harm' can become 'pure benefit' by context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما كان ضرراً محضاً فليس بمشروع في حقه، فبطلت مباشرته، وذلك مثل الطلاق والعتاق والهبة والقرض والصدقة، ولم يملك ذلك عليه غيره، ما خلا القرض، فإنه يملكه القاضي عليه؛ لأن صيانة الحقوق لما كانت بولاية القضاء : انقلب القرض بحال القضاء نفعاً محضاً،
And as for what is pure harm, it is not legislated in his right, so his direct performance of it is void. And that is such as divorce, freeing slaves, gift, loan, and charity. And no one else has authority over him regarding that, except the loan, for the judge has authority over him regarding it; because the preservation of rights, since it is by the jurisdiction of the judiciary, the loan by the situation of the judiciary becomes pure benefit.
Source pages p. 719

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on actions that are pure harm (darar mahd) for a minor or slave. Such actions are not legislated as valid from them—they are void. Examples include divorce (talaq), emancipation (itaq), gift (hiba), loan (qard), and charity (sadaqa). No other person (like a guardian) can compel the minor/slave to perform these. However, a loan (qard) is an exception: the judge (qadi) may compel the minor/slave to take a loan because, under judicial authority, the loan becomes a pure benefit (e.g., to preserve rights or pay debts). The reasoning is that the act of lending money, when ordered by a judge to secure rights, transforms into a beneficial act for the minor/slave, so it is allowed.

Key Terms
ضرر محض (darar mahd): An action that causes pure harm to the agent without any countervailing benefit, e.g., divorce liberates a wife and imposes financial obligations, freeing a slave loses property without return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When can a judge compel a minor to take a loan?
A) Always
B) Only when it becomes pure benefit (e.g., to pay essential debts)
C) Never
D) Only with the guardian's consent

Key Takeaway
Pure harm actions (darar mahd) by a minor or slave are void and cannot be compelled by anyone except a judge may compel a loan when it becomes beneficial.

Classifying Transactions for Interdicted Minors and Slaves
Classifies transactions into pure benefit, pure harm, and mixed for the interdicted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify transactions for an interdicted minor or slave into pure benefit, pure harm, and mixed, and apply the Hanafi ruling on each type, citing the relevant proof and istihsan-based exceptions.

Core Idea
Interdicted minors and slaves may validly perform only transactions of pure benefit without guardian permission.

In Hanafi usul, the capacity of an interdicted person (محجور) is assessed by the nature of the transaction itself. Al-Bazdawi introduces a tripartite classification: pure benefit (نفع محض), pure harm (ضرر محض), and transactions that oscillate between benefit and harm. By understanding this classification, you will be able to determine which acts a minor or slave can perform independently, which require a guardian or judge, and which are void. The passage also highlights the role of istihsan in providing concessions for benefit, such as allowing wages for labor performed without permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Note that transactions are divided into pure benefit, pure harm, and those that combine benefit and harm (the mixed category is implied in the examples).
Pure benefit includes accepting gifts; pure harm includes divorce; mixed includes hiring oneself out.
2
Apply the rule to pure benefit transactions
For pure benefit (e.g., accepting a gift, accepting charity, accepting the substitute for khul'), the interdicted person may act validly even without guardian permission. This is because the act contains only benefit.
Note the exception for a slave's hiring: safety is a condition, per istihsan.
3
Apply the rule to pure harm transactions
Pure harm transactions (divorce, manumission, gift, loan, charity) are not legislated for the interdicted person; their direct action is void, and no one can compel them except in the case of a loan by a judge, which transforms into pure benefit through judicial authority.
The loan exception shows how context can change the classification.
4
Analyze the mixed transactions via istihsan
For acts like hiring oneself out or fighting without permission (mixed benefit/harm), istihsan allows the obligation of wages or compensation if benefit accrues, provided conditions (e.g., safety for a slave) are met.
This illustrates the Hanafi method of giving preference to benefit when no explicit text exists.
5
Verify the proof from Quran and reasoning
Al-Bazdawi cites Surah al-Rahman 4 ('He taught him speech') to honor the minor's capacity for valid speech in agency, and Surah al-Nisa 6 ('Test the orphans') to justify allowing minors to engage in transactions for learning. Both support the validity of beneficial utterances.
The proofs are based on general meanings (zanni al-dalala) but are qat'i in transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكذلك ما هو نفع محض من التصرفات مثل قبول الهبة، وقبول الصدقة، وذلك مثل قبولِ بدل الخلع من العبد المحجور بغير إذن المولى، فإنه يصح. وكذلك إذا آجَرَ الصبيُّ المحجورُ أو العبد المحجور نفسه، ومضى على ذلك العمل: وجب الأجر للحر؛ استحساناً؛ لما فيه من النفع، ووجب للعبد بشرط السلامة ولا تشترط السلامة في الصبي الحر. وكذلك العبد إذا قاتل بغير إذن المولى، والصبي بغير إذن الولي: استوجب الرضحَ؛ استحساناً.
ويحتمل أن يكون هذا قول محمد رحمه الله، فإنه لم يُذكر إلا في السير الكبير».
ووجب القول بصحة عبارة الصبي في بيع مال غيره، وطلاق غيره، وعتاق غيره إذا كان وكيلاً؛ لأن الآدمي مكرَّم لصحة العبارة، وعِلْم البيان قال الله تعالى: ﴿عَلَّمَهُ الْبَيَانَ ﴾ الرحمن / ٤ ، فكان القول بصحته من أعظم المنافع الخالصة.
وفي ذلك توصل إلى درك المضار والمنافع، واهتداء في التجارة بالتجربة، قال الله تعالى : وابْتَلُوا اليَنَمَى النساء / ٦ .
وأما ما كان ضرراً محضاً فليس بمشروع في حقه، فبطلت مباشرته، وذلك مثل الطلاق والعتاق والهبة والقرض والصدقة، ولم يملك ذلك عليه غيره، ما خلا القرض، فإنه يملكه القاضي عليه؛ لأن صيانة الحقوق لما كانت بولاية القضاء : انقلب القرض بحال القضاء نفعاً محضاً، لا تشوبه مضرة؛ لأن العين غير مأمون العطب، والدين مأمون العطب، إلا من قِبَل التَّوَى، وقد وقع الأمنُ عنه بولاية القضاء، فصار ملحقاً بهذا الشرط بالمنافع الخالصة.
Similarly, what is pure benefit from transactions, such as accepting a gift, accepting charity, and like that such as accepting the substitute for khul' from an interdicted slave without the master's permission, it is valid. Likewise, if an interdicted minor or an interdicted slave hires himself out and the work is performed: the wage is obligatory for the free person by istihsan due to the benefit in it, and it is obligatory for the slave subject to safety, and safety is not a condition for the free minor. Likewise, the slave if he fights without the master's permission, and the minor without the guardian's permission: he becomes entitled to compensation by istihsan. And it is possible that this is the saying of Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, for it is only mentioned in al-Siyar al-Kabir. And it is necessary to affirm the validity of the minor's speech in selling the property of another, divorcing another's wife, and manumitting another's slave if he is an agent, because the human is honored for the validity of speech and the knowledge of expression. Allah said: 'He taught him speech' (al-Rahman 4). So affirming its validity is among the greatest pure benefits. And in that is a means to perceive harms and benefits, and guidance in trade through experience. Allah said: 'And test the orphans' (al-Nisa 6). As for what is pure harm, it is not legislated for him, so his direct undertaking is void, such as divorce, manumission, gift, loan, and charity. And no one else has the authority to compel him to do that, except the loan, for the judge has authority over him regarding it, because the protection of rights is through the authority of judgment: the loan with the status of judgment becomes pure benefit, untainted by harm, because the physical object is not safe from destruction, while the debt is safe from destruction except from insolvency, and security from that is achieved through the authority of judgment, so it becomes attached to this condition of pure benefits.
Source pages p. 719

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul establishes the principle that the legal capacity of an interdicted minor (صبي محجور) or slave (عبد محجور) is determined by the nature of the transaction in terms of benefit and harm. The author classifies transactions into three categories: pure benefit (نفع محض), pure harm (ضرر محض), and those that oscillate between benefit and harm (ما يتردد بين النفع والضرر). For pure benefit transactions, such as accepting a gift or charity, or even accepting the substitute (badal) in khul', the interdicted person may perform them validly even without guardian permission, because they entail only benefit. For a minor or slave who hires himself out, the wage is due: for the free minor unconditionally, for the slave only if he comes to no harm (safety condition), and the slave's fighting without permission also leads to entitlement to compensation (رَضْخ) by istihsan. The author notes that this ruling is attributed to Muhammad al-Shaybani and found in al-Siyar al-Kabir. For pure harm transactions, like divorce, manumission, gift, loan, and charity, they are void because they cause only harm; the interdicted person cannot be compelled by others except in the case of a loan, where the judge may impose it because through the judge's authority the loan becomes pure benefit (the creditor's claim is safer than the physical item). The passage also affirms that a minor's speech (عبارة) is valid in agency because of the honor of speech and the Quranic verse 'He taught him speech.'

Key Terms
نفع محض (naf' mahd): A transaction that entails only benefit with no element of harm for the interdicted person.
ضرر محض (darar mahd): A transaction that entails only harm for the interdicted person, thus void if performed.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference used to derive rulings that better serve benefit, here justifying compensation for labor despite lack of permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is considered pure harm (ضرر محض) for an interdicted minor, and thus invalid if performed by him without guardian permission?
A) Accepting a gift
B) Divorce
C) Selling property as an agent
D) Hiring himself out

Key Takeaway
An interdicted person's capacity to transact depends on the nature of the act: pure benefit acts are valid without permission, pure harm acts are void, and mixed acts may be validated by istihsan for benefit.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Minor's Capacity: Pure Benefit, Mixed Transactions, and Guardian's Permission
Explaining minor's capacity based on benefit/harm classification and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a minor's transaction as pure benefit, pure harm, or mixed benefit/harm, and apply the Hanafi ruling that pure benefit transactions are valid without guardian permission, while mixed transactions require guardian approval.

Core Idea
A minor may own pure benefit without guardian; mixed transactions need guardian's opinion to remove harm.

This lesson explores the foundational classification in Hanafi usul regarding a minor's legal capacity. The text establishes that acts of pure benefit are valid for a minor alone, acts of pure harm are invalid, and acts that waver between benefit and harm require guardian permission. We then examine the intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of gross fraud (ghabn fahish) on a minor's sale with guardian permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of transactions for a minor
From the text, note that transactions are classified as pure benefit (e.g., gifts, charity), pure harm (e.g., incurring debt), or mixed benefit and harm (sale, lease, marriage). The minor's capacity differs for each.
The text uses 'ما يتردد بين النفع والضرر' for mixed.
2
State the ruling for each category
Pure benefit: the minor may own it alone. Pure harm: he may not own it. Mixed: he may own it only with the guardian's opinion (ra'y al-wali).
The guardian's opinion removes the probability of harm.
3
Understand the justification for mixed transactions
The guardian's opinion compensates the minor's deficiency in judgment, making him like an adult. The text says 'احتمال الضرر في التصرف يزول برأي الولي، حتى يُجعل الصبي كالبالغ'.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on ghabn fahish
Abu Hanifa: the sale is valid even with gross fraud from strangers because the guardian's opinion makes the minor fully competent. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: the sale is invalid because the guardian's opinion is a condition for permissibility, and the transaction is treated as if the guardian himself executed it, so gross fraud invalidates it.
There are also two narrations from Abu Hanifa regarding transactions with the guardian himself.
5
Apply the principle to agency
The text extends the principle: if a legally incapacitated person (محجور) acts as an agent, liability does not bind him without guardian permission; with permission, it binds.
This shows consistency in the Hanafi approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا تشوبه مضرة؛ لأن العين غير مأمون العطب، والدين مأمون العطب، إلا من قِبَل التَّوَى، وقد وقع الأمنُ عنه بولاية القضاء، فصار ملحقاً بهذا الشرط بالمنافع الخالصة. وأما ما يتردَّد بين النفع والضرر مثل البيع والإجارة والنكاح، وما أشبه ذلك: فإنه لا يملكه بنفسه؛ لما فيه من الاحتمال، ويملكه برأي الولي ; لأنه أهل لحكمه بمباشرة الولي، وقد صار أهلاً تُتصوّر المباشرة ، فإذا صار أهلاً للحكم : كان أهلاً للسبب لا محالة. منه وفي القول بصحة مباشرته برأي الولي إصابة، مثل ما يصاب بمباشرة الولي، لا محالة، مع فضل نفع البيان، وتوسع طريق الإصابة، وذلك بطريق أن احتمال الضرر في التصرف يزول برأي الولي، حتى يُجعل الصبي كالبالغ ، وذلك في قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. ألا يُرى أنه صحح بيعه بغَبْنِ فاحش من الأجانب، والولي لا يملكه، وذلك باعتبار أن نقصان رأيه جُبر برأي الولي، فصار كالبالغ. وعند أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله بطريق أن رأي الولي شَرْم الجواز، وعموم رأيه كخصوصه، فيُجعل كأن الولي باشره بنفسه. ولذلك قالا : لا يملكه بالغبن الفاحش مع الأجانب، ومع الولي. وعن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله في التصرف مع الولي بالغبن الفاحش روايتان في رواية : أجازه ; لما قلنا وفي رواية : ردَّه بشرط النيابة، وذلك أنه الملك أصيل، وفي الرأي أصيل من وجه دون وجه، ألا يُرى أن له أصل الرأي، دون وصفه فتثبت شبهة النيابة، فاعتبرت في موضع التهمة، وسقطت في غير موضع التهمة. وعلى هذا الأصل قلنا: إن المحجور إذا توكل: لم تلزمه العهدة، وبإذن الولي: تلزمه. وأما إذا أوصى الصبي بشيء من وصايا البر: بطلت وصيته عندنا وإن كان فيها نفع ظاهر ; لأن الإرث شرع نفعاً للمورث، ألا يُرى أنه شرع في حق الصبي، وفي الانتقال إلى الإيصاء: تَرْكُ الأفضل، لا محالة، إلا أنه مشروع في حق البالغ ، كما شرع له الطلاق في النكاح، ولم يُشرع في حق الصغير، فكذلك هذا. ولذلك قلنا: لا يجوز أن يُخيّر الصبي بين الأبوين بعد الفرقة; لأنه من يتردد بين الضرر والنفع والغالبُ من حاله الميل إلى الهوى والشهوة، والولي في موضع النزاع ليس بولي، فبطل اختياره. وقد خالفنا الشافعي رحمه الله في هذه الجملة خلافاً متناقضاً، لا يستقيم على شيء من أصول الفقه، وكفى به حجةً عليه، ولم يُعتدَّ بخلافه; لأنه قد قال بصحة كثير من عباراته في اختيار أحد الأبوين، وفي الإيصاء، وفي العبادات، وقال بلزوم الإحرام من غير نفع، وأبطل الإيمان وهو نفع محض. وليس له فقه في شيء من ذلك إلا شيئاً موضوعاً، وهو أن مَن كان مولياً عليه لا يصلح ولياً; لأن أحدهما: سمة العجز، والثاني: آيةً القدرة وهما متضادان، فأجرى هذا الأصل في الفروع، وطرده بلا فقه معقول، فقال يصح اختياره أحد الأبوين، ولا يصح اختيار الولي عليه.
Free from any harm; because chattel is not safe from damage, while debt is safe from damage, except from destruction, and safety from it occurred by the authority of the judiciary, so it became attached by this condition to pure benefits. As for what wavers between benefit and harm, such as sale, lease, marriage, and the like: he does not own it by himself because of the probability [of harm], and he owns it by the opinion of the guardian because he is qualified for its judgement through the guardian's execution, and he becomes qualified such that execution is conceivable; so when he becomes qualified for judgement, he becomes qualified for the cause necessarily. Therefrom: And in the statement of the validity of his execution by the guardian's opinion there is correctness, like what is attained by the guardian's execution necessarily, with the added benefit of clarification and expansion of the path of correctness; and that is by way that the probability of harm in the transaction is removed by the guardian's opinion, so that the minor is made like an adult. And that is according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. Do you not see that he upheld his sale with gross fraud from strangers, while the guardian does not own it? And that is by considering that his deficiency in judgement was compensated by the guardian's opinion, so he became like an adult. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, by way that the guardian's opinion is a condition for permissibility, and the generality of his opinion is like its specificity, so it is as if the guardian himself executed it. Therefore they said: He does not own it with gross fraud with strangers, nor with the guardian. And from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, regarding the transaction with the guardian with gross fraud, there are two narrations: in one narration: he permitted it; for what we said; and in another narration: he rejected it with the condition of deputyship. And that is because he is an original owner, and in judgement he is original in one respect but not another; do you not see that he has the foundation of judgement but not its perfection? So the semblance of deputyship is established, and it is considered in a place of suspicion, and drops in a place not of suspicion. And upon this principle we said: If a legally incapacitated person acts as an agent, the liability does not bind him; but with the guardian's permission it binds him. As for if a minor makes a bequest of something from charitable bequests: his bequest is invalid according to us even if it contains apparent benefit; because inheritance is legislated as benefit for the testator; do you not see that it is legislated in favor of the minor, and in turning to bequest there is leaving the better, necessarily, except that it is legislated in favor of the adult, just as divorce is legislated for him in marriage, and it is not legislated for the minor; so likewise this. And therefore we said: It is not permissible for a minor to be given choice between the parents after separation; because he wavers between harm and benefit, and his usual state is inclination towards desire and passion, and the guardian in a place of dispute is not a guardian, so his choice is invalid. And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, has opposed us in this entire matter with a contradictory opposition that is not consistent with any of the principles of fiqh, and it suffices as a proof against him, and his opposition is not considered; because he has upheld the validity of many of his statements in choosing one of the parents, in bequest, and in acts of worship, and he has declared the obligation of ihram without benefit, and invalidated faith while it is pure benefit. And he has no fiqh in any of that except a fabricated thing: that whoever is under guardianship is not fit to be a guardian; because one is a mark of incapacity, and the second is a sign of capability, and they are contradictory. So he applied this principle to the branches and extended it without meaningful fiqh, saying that a minor's choice of one parent is valid, but the choice of a guardian over him is invalid.
Source pages p. 720

Commentary
This passage is a usul analysis of the legal capacity of minors (صبي) in various transactions, applying the principle that capacity depends on whether the action involves pure benefit, pure harm, or mixed benefit and harm. The text begins by stating that a minor may own property that is pure benefit without any associated harm (e.g., gifts, charity) because the risk is minimal. It then contrasts transactions like sale, lease, and marriage, which waver between benefit and harm; such transactions are not valid when executed by the minor alone, but become valid with the guardian's (ولي) opinion. The reasoning is that the guardian's judgement removes the probability of harm, elevating the minor to the status of an adult for that purpose. The text then details a dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the effect of gross fraud (غبن فاحش) in a minor's sale. Abu Hanifa holds that once the guardian permits, the minor's transaction is valid even if there is gross fraud with a stranger; he considers the guardian's opinion as compensating the minor's deficiency. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that the guardian's opinion is a condition for permissibility (شرط الجواز) and that the transaction is treated as if the guardian himself executed it, so gross fraud invalidates it even with a stranger. There are two narrations from Abu Hanifa regarding transactions with the guardian himself. The text then applies this principle to agency (وكالة): a legally incapacitated person (محجور) who acts as an agent is not liable unless the guardian permits. Regarding bequest (وصية) by a minor, it is invalid even if charitable, because inheritance is legislated for benefit, and a will is considered a departure from the superior method (inheritance); just as divorce is not allowed for minors. Finally, the text discusses the minor's choice between parents after divorce: it is not allowed because the minor is swayed by desire, and the guardian is not a true guardian in such a dispute. The passage concludes with a harsh critique of al-Shafi'i, accusing him of contradictions: he allows a minor's choice in some matters but disallows in others, and his reasoning is inconsistent with usul principles.

Key Terms
الصبي (al-sabi): A minor who has not reached puberty and is under legal guardianship.
الولي (al-wali): The guardian who manages the minor's affairs and provides consent for his transactions.
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghabn al-fahish): Excessive fraud or significant disparity in value in a transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a minor sells an item to a stranger with the guardian's permission but with gross fraud (غبن فاحش), the sale is:
A) Valid, because the guardian's opinion compensates the minor's deficiency, making him like an adult.
B) Invalid, because the minor cannot be held liable for gross fraud regardless of permission.
C) Valid only if the guardian himself is present at the sale.
D) Invalid, because gross fraud with a stranger is always prohibited.

Key Takeaway
A minor's capacity in transactions is determined by the balance of benefit and harm: pure benefit acts are valid without guardian, mixed acts require guardian, and pure harm acts are invalid.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
10 min
The Minor's Choice, Bequest, Agency, and Refutation of al-Shafi'i
Applying benefit/harm principle to agency, bequest, and choice; refuting al-Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings regarding a minor's agency, bequest, and choice between parents, and evaluate al-Shafi'i's inconsistent position using the benefit/harm principle.

Core Idea
A minor's bequest and choice between parents are invalid because they waver between benefit and harm, while al-Shafi'i's contradictory rulings betray a flawed usul.

In this lesson, we apply the benefit/harm classification to specific cases: a minor's agency, bequest, and choice between parents after divorce. The text then launches a strong critique of al-Shafi'i, accusing him of inconsistency. We will examine the Hanafi rationale and the Shafi'i counter-argument, and understand why the Hanafi position is considered more coherent.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling for a minor's agency
If a minor acts as an agent without guardian permission, the liability does not bind him. With guardian permission, it binds. This is based on the principle that the minor's capacity is partial.
The text says 'إن المحجور إذا توكل: لم تلزمه العهدة، وبإذن الولي: تلزمه'.
2
State the ruling for a minor's bequest
A minor's bequest (even charitable) is invalid. The reasoning is that inheritance is the superior method of transferring wealth; a bequest is a departure from that, and the minor cannot choose to leave the better method.
Analogous to divorce: not legislated for minors.
3
State the ruling for a minor's choice between parents
It is not permissible to give the minor a choice after divorce because he wavers between benefit and harm, is prone to desire, and the guardian in a dispute is not a true guardian.
The guardian is replaced by the judge in disputes, so the minor's choice is void.
4
Present al-Shafi'i's position and the Hanafi critique
Al-Shafi'i allows the minor's choice in selecting a parent but invalidates it in other contexts (like agency). His basis: a person under guardianship cannot be a guardian. The Hanafi response: this is contradictory; he has no consistent usul.
The text calls his opposition 'متناقضاً' (contradictory) and 'موضوعاً' (fabricated).
5
Analyze the Hanafi refutation
The Hanafi school argues that the benefit/harm classification is the correct principle. Al-Shafi'i's rule is applied inconsistently: he allows the minor's choice in one case but not another, showing lack of deep understanding (fiqh).
The text says 'ليس له فقه في شيء من ذلك إلا شيئاً موضوعاً'.

الإيصاء (al-isa'): Bequest; a will made by a person regarding property after death.

According to Hanafi law, a minor's bequest (وصية) for a charitable purpose is:
A) Valid, because charity is pure benefit.
B) Invalid, because bequest is a departure from the superior method of inheritance.
C) Valid only if the guardian approves.

Key Takeaway
A minor's bequest and choice between parents are invalid in Hanafi law because they involve decisions that waver between benefit and harm and require full legal capacity.

The Hanafi Principle of Probability (Ihtimal) in Causes: The Case of a Minor's Gift Acceptance
Analyzes the ruling on a minor's acceptance of a gift and the role of probability in causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school accepts probability (ihtimal) in the cause for a minor's acceptance of a gift and identify the two competing considerations.

Core Idea
A minor's dual capacity creates probability; Hanafis embrace it for ruling completeness.

In this passage, al-Bazdawi addresses a difference of opinion regarding a minor's acceptance of a gift. He introduces the principle of probability (ihtimal) as the key to reconciling seemingly conflicting rulings. By examining the minor's dual legal capacity, you will see how Hanafi usul permits a cause to have two aspects, ensuring that the ruling achieves its intended completeness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions
One view says the minor's acceptance is valid without the guardian; another says the opposite. Note both positions from the text.
2
Analyze the minor's dual capacity
The minor is qāṣir al-ahliyyah (deficient capacity) yet has aṣl al-ahliyyah (original capacity). Understand how both aspects coexist.
3
Reconcile through probability
The Hanafi school does not choose one view absolutely; it accepts the probability (iḥtimāl) in the cause itself, allowing flexibility.
This probability is expressed as 'itarat al-umūr'.
4
Understand the rationale
The purpose of causes is to achieve their rulings; accepting iḥtimāl ensures the ruling is complete and serves the Sharīʿah's intent.
The passage ends with the maxim 'al-umūr bi-ʿawāqibihā'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك قبول الهبة في قول: يصح منه، دون الولي، وفي قول: عكسه، ولا فقه فيه؛ لأنه لم يَبْن الأمر على دليل الصحة والعدم من الصبي. وعندنا : لما كان قاصر الأهلية: صلح مولياً عليه، ولما كان صاحبَ أصل الأهلية: صلح ولياً، ومتى جعلناه ولياً: لم نجعله فيه مولياً عليه، وإذا جعلناه مولياً عليه: لم نجعله ولياً فيه. وإنما هذا عبارة عن الاحتمال وهو راجع إلى توسع طريق النيل والإصابة، وذلك هو المقصود ؛ لأن المقصود من الأسباب أحكامها، فوجب احتمال هذا التردد في السبب ؛ لسلامة الحكم على الكمال، وإنما الأمور بعواقبها، والله أعلم.
Similarly, the acceptance of a gift: in one opinion, it is valid from him, not from the guardian; and in another opinion, the opposite. There is no independent legal reasoning (fiqh) in it, because the matter was not built upon evidence of validity or invalidity from the minor. According to us: since he is deficient in legal capacity, he is suitable to be under guardianship; and since he has original capacity, he is suitable to be a guardian. When we make him a guardian in one matter, we do not make him under guardianship in it; and when we make him under guardianship, we do not make him a guardian in it. This is an expression of probability, which is referred to expanding the method of attainment and hitting the mark. That is the intent, because the intent of causes is their rulings, so this probability of indecision in the cause must be accepted for the completeness of the ruling. Matters are judged by their consequences, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 722

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi position on a minor's acceptance of a gift. There is a difference of opinion: one view holds the minor's acceptance is valid without the guardian, another says the opposite. The Hanafi school reconciles by considering the minor's dual nature: he is deficient in capacity (thus subject to guardianship) but also has original capacity (thus can act as a guardian in some contexts). The key principle is that the ruling is built on probability (iḥtimāl) in the cause, which expands the method of reaching correct judgments. The intent of the Sharīʿah is to preserve the completeness of rulings, and this is achieved by accommodating such probability. The final comment reminds that matters are judged by their consequences.

Key Terms
قاصر الأهلية (qāṣir al-ahliyyah): One whose legal capacity is deficient, such as a minor of discernment.
احتمال (iḥtimāl): Probability; the possibility of two conflicting considerations in a cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented, what is the reason for accepting probability (ihtimal) in the cause for a minor's acceptance of a gift?
A) To preserve the completeness of the ruling
B) Because the minor has no capacity
C) Because the guardian's consent is required
D) Because the gift is revocable

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi acceptance of ihtimal in causes allows for flexible rulings that fulfill the Sharīʿah's intent, as seen in the minor's gift acceptance.

Classification of Impediments to Legal Capacity and the Ruling on Insanity
Defines the two types of impediments to legal capacity and applies qiyas and istihsan to insanity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify impediments to legal capacity into celestial and acquired, and explain the Hanafi ruling on insanity using qiyas and istihsan.

Core Idea
Insanity negates capacity by qiyas, but brief episodes are excused by istihsan like sleep.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul introduces the pivotal Hanafi concept of impediments to legal capacity (awāriḍ al-ahliyyah). These impediments affect a person's ability to perform acts of worship and transactions. The text first categorizes them as celestial (beyond human control) and acquired (from oneself or others), then zooms in on insanity (junūn) to illustrate how strict analogy (qiyās) is tempered by juristic preference (istiḥsān). Understanding this interplay is essential for grasping Hanafi legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of impediments
The passage divides impediments into celestial (samāwī) and acquired (muktasab). List the specific impediments under each category as mentioned in the text.
Memorize the lists: celestial includes 11 items; acquired includes two subcategories.
2
Understand the qiyas ruling on insanity
By analogy (qiyās), insanity invalidates all obligations because it negates the capacity to perform. Trace the reasoning: insanity contradicts ability → performance absent → obligation absent.
The qiyās is based on the principle that obligation depends on capacity.
3
Apply istihsan to modify the qiyas
The Hanafi scholars applied istiḥsān to exempt short-term insanity (before duration). They treat it like sleep or unconsciousness, where obligations are excused.
Istiḥsān overrides qiyās due to avoidance of hardship (ḥaraj).
4
Note the rationale for the exception
Brief insanity does not cause unreasonable hardship, unlike prolonged insanity. The reference to prophets being protected from insanity underscores the severity of the condition.
The rationale is rooted in the principle of ease (taysīr).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الأمراض المعترضة على الأهلية العوارض نوعان: سماوي، ومكتسب. وأما السماوي فهو الصَّغَرُ، والجنون والعته، والنسيان، والنوم، والإغماء، والرّق، والمرض، والحيض والنفاس، والموت. وأما المكتسب فإنه نوعان منه، ومن غيره. أما الذي منه : فالجهل، والسكر، والهَزْل، والسَّفَهُ، والخطأ، والسفر. وأما الذي من غيره : فالإكراه بما فيه إلجاء، وبما ليس فيه إلجاء. أما (الجنون) : فإنه في القياس مُسقِط للعبادات كلها؛ لأنه ينافي القدرة، فينعدم به الأداء، فينعدم الوجوب؛ لانعدامه، لكنهم استحسنوا فيه إذا زال قبل الامتداد، فجعلوه عفواً، وألحقوه بالنوم والإغماء، وذلك أنه لما كان منافياً لأهلية الأداء : كان القياس فيه ما قلنا، ألا يُرى أن الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام عصموا عنه. لكنه إذا لم يمتد : لم يكن موجباً حرجاً على ما قلنا.
Chapter on Impediments to Legal Capacity. Impediments are of two types: celestial and acquired. As for celestial: minority, insanity, imbecility, forgetfulness, sleep, unconsciousness, slavery, illness, menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and death. As for acquired, it is of two types: from oneself and from others. As for from oneself: ignorance, intoxication, jest, prodigality, error, and travel. As for from others: coercion with compulsion and without compulsion. As for insanity: in qiyās, it invalidates all acts of worship, because it contradicts ability, so performance is absent, so obligation is absent due to its absence. But they (the scholars) applied istiḥsān in its regard: if it ceases before duration, they consider it excused and treat it like sleep and unconsciousness. This is because, since it contradicts the capacity for performance, the qiyās was as we said. Do you not see that prophets (peace be upon them) are protected from it? However, if it does not persist, it does not cause hardship as we said.
Source pages p. 723

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on impediments to legal capacity (awāriḍ al-ahliyyah). It divides them into two major categories: celestial (samāwī) and acquired (muktasab). Celestial impediments are beyond human control, such as minority, insanity, imbecility, forgetfulness, sleep, etc. Acquired impediments come from human action or external compulsion. The text then focuses on insanity (junūn): by strict analogy (qiyās), insanity invalidates all obligations of worship because it negates the capacity to perform. However, the Hanafi scholars applied istiḥsān (juristic preference) to exempt short-term insanity that does not extend over a period, treating it like sleep or unconsciousness. This is based on the principle that such brief insanity does not cause undue hardship. The mention of prophets being protected from insanity indicates the seriousness of the condition.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning; the rule that insanity removes obligation.
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Juristic preference; overriding qiyās due to subtle reasoning or necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, what is the effect of insanity that ceases quickly?
A) It invalidates all past acts
B) It is excused like sleep
C) It requires making up missed prayers
D) It removes legal capacity permanently

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses istiḥsān to soften the strict qiyās on insanity, excusing brief episodes to avoid hardship.

Classification of Impediments and the Ruling on Temporary Madness
Classifying impediments and applying qiyas/istihsan to madness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between heavenly and acquired impediments, and apply the Hanafi rule that temporary madness does not nullify obligation but pardons performance via istihsan.

Core Idea
Temporary madness pardons missed worship via istihsan, but obligation remains.

This passage introduces the classification of impediments affecting legal capacity and then focuses on madness (junūn). By strict analogy, madness would nullify all acts of worship because it eliminates the capacity to perform. However, the Hanafi school applies istihsan to pardon short-term madness, preventing hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Types of Impediments
Read the first part of the passage and list the two categories: heavenly and acquired. Note examples for each.
Heavenly impediments are involuntary; acquired are from oneself or others.
2
Understand the Qiyas Ruling on Madness
Analyze the qiyas reasoning: madness negates capacity for performance, so both performance and obligation are nullified.
This is the strict analogy.
3
Recognize the Istihsan Exception
Identify the istihsan: if madness is temporary (before extension), it is pardoned, like sleep or fainting. This avoids hardship.
Hardship (haraj) is the key justification.
4
Note the Disagreement on Original vs. Accidental Madness
Compare Abu Yusuf's position (original madness treated differently) with Muhammad's (both same).
This affects how a minor who reaches puberty insane is treated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الأمور المعترضة على الأهلية
العوارض نوعان: سماوي، ومكتسب.
وأما السماوي فهو الصَّغَرُ، والجنون والعته، والنسيان، والنوم، والإغماء، والرّق، والمرض، والحيض والنفاس، والموت.
وأما المكتسب فإنه نوعان منه، ومن غيره.
وأما الذي منه : فالجهل، والسكر، والهَزْل، والسَّفَهُ، والخطأ، والسفر.
وأما الذي من غيره : فالإكراه بما فيه إلجاء، وبما ليس فيه إلجاء.
أما الجنون : فإنه في القياس مُسقِط للعبادات كلها؛ لأنه ينافي القدرة، فينعدم به الأداء، فينعدم الوجوب؛ لانعدامه، لكنهم استحسنوا فيه إذا زال قبل الامتداد، فجعلوه عفواً، وألحقوه بالنوم والإغماء، وذلك أنه لما كان منافياً لأهلية الأداء : كان القياس فيه ما قلنا، ألا يُرى أن الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام عصموا عنه.
لكنه إذا لم يمتد : لم يكن موجباً حرجاً على ما قلنا.
وقد اختلفوا فيه، فقال أبو يوسف رحمه الله : هذا إذا كان عارضاً غير أصلي ؛ ليُلحق بالعوارض، فأما إذا بلغ الصبي مجنوناً : فإذا زال: صار في معنى الصبي إذا بلغ.
وقال محمد رحمه الله : هو والأول سواء، واعتبر حاله فيما يزول عنه، ويُلحق بأصله.
وهو في أصل الخلقة متفاوت بين جنون مديد وقصيرٍ، فيُلحَقُ هذا الأصل في الحكم الذي لم يستوعبه بالعارض، وذلك في الجنون الأصلي إذا زال قبل انسلاخ شهر رمضان.
وحد الامتداد يختلف باختلاف الطاعات:
فأما في الصلوات: فبأن يزيد على يوم وليلة باعتبار الصلوات عند محمد رحمه الله ؛ لتصير ستاً، فيدخل في حد التكرار. وأقام أبو حنيفة وأبو يوسف رحمهما الله الوقت فيه مقامَ الصلاة؛ تيسيراً، فاعتبرا الزيادة بالساعات.
وفي الصوم: بأن يَستغرق شهر رمضان، ولم يُعتبر التكرار ؛ لأن ذلك لا يثبت إلا بحول.
وفي الزكاة بأن يستغرق الحول عند محمد رحمه الله، وأقام أبو يوسف رحمه الله أكثر الحولِ مقامَ كله فيما يمتد؛ عملاً بالتيسير والتخفيف.
فإذا زال قبل هذا الحد وهو أصلي : كان على هذا الاختلاف.
وقد بينا من قبلُ أن الجنونَ لا ينافي أهلية الوجوب؛ لأنه لا ينافي
Chapter on Matters That Affect Legal Capacity. The impediments are of two types: heavenly and acquired. As for the heavenly, they are: childhood, madness, imbecility, forgetfulness, sleep, fainting, slavery, illness, menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and death. As for the acquired, they are two types: from oneself and from others. As for that from oneself: ignorance, intoxication, jest, foolishness, error, and travel. As for that from others: coercion with compulsion and without compulsion. As for madness: by analogy it cancels all acts of worship, because it contradicts capacity, so performance is nullified, and obligation is nullified due to its non-existence. However, they exercised istihsan in this case when it disappears before extension, so they made it a pardon, and attached it to sleep and fainting. That is because since it contradicts the capacity of performance, by analogy it is as we said—do you not see that the prophets (peace and blessings be upon them) were protected from it. But when it does not extend, it does not cause hardship, as we said. They differed on this: Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) said this is when it is an accidental impediment, to be attached to the impediments. But if a minor reaches puberty as a madman, then when it disappears, he becomes in the same meaning as a minor who reaches puberty. Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: he and the first case are equal, and he considers his condition in what he emerges from, and attaches it to his origin. And in the original constitution of creation, it varies between prolonged and short madness, so this origin is attached to the ruling that is not encompassed by the impediment, and that is in original madness when it disappears before the end of the month of Ramadan. The limit of extension differs according to acts of worship: As for prayers: according to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), it is by exceeding a day and a night, considering the prayers, so that they become six, thus entering the limit of repetition. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on them) considered time in place of the prayer for ease, so they considered increase in hours. As for fasting: by encompassing the month of Ramadan, and repetition is not considered because that is only established by a year. As for zakat: by encompassing the year according to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), and Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) considered most of the year in place of all of it in what extends, acting on ease and leniency. If it disappears before this limit and it is original, then this is according to this difference. And we have already explained before that madness does not contradict the capacity of obligation, because it does not contradict.
Source pages p. 723

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on impediments (al-‘awāriḍ) that affect legal capacity (al-ahliyya). The author classifies impediments into two main types: heavenly (samaawī) which are involuntary and beyond human control, and acquired (muktasab) which are voluntary or caused by human action. The heavenly impediments include childhood, madness, imbecility, forgetfulness, sleep, fainting, slavery, illness, menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and death. The acquired are subdivided into those from oneself (ignorance, intoxication, jest, foolishness, error, travel) and those from others (coercion with or without compulsion). The author then focuses on madness (junūn) as a heavenly impediment. By strict analogy (qiyās), madness would nullify all acts of worship because it negates the capacity for performance (ahliyya al-adā’), thereby nullifying performance and hence obligation. However, the Hanafi scholars applied istiḥsān (juristic preference) to treat temporary madness that disappears before extending beyond a certain limit as pardoned, likening it to sleep and fainting. This is because short-term madness, like sleep, does not cause excessive hardship. The scholars differed on the details: Abu Yusuf distinguished between accidental madness (original) and madness occurring in a minor who reaches puberty already mad, treating the latter like a minor attaining sanity. Muhammad considered both cases the same, linking the ruling to the origin of the madness. The text then explains that the limit of extension (imtidād) varies by act of worship. For prayers, according to Muhammad, the madness must extend beyond a day and a night so that six prayers are missed, which enters the criterion of repetition (takrār). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, for ease, consider increase in hours as the threshold. For fasting, the limit is covering the entire month of Ramadan; repetition is not a factor since a full month is already a unit. For zakāt, Muhammad requires the madness to cover the entire year (ḥawal), while Abu Yusuf considers most of the year sufficient for continuous obligations, again for ease. If the madness is original and disappears before these limits, the rulings differ. Finally, the author reiterates that madness does not negate the capacity for obligation (ahliyya al-wujūb), because it does not negate the subjecthood required for obligation—only performance is affected.

Key Terms
الجنون (al-junūn): عارض سماوي يعدم أهلية الأداء دون أهلية الوجوب، ويعفى عنه إذا زال قبل الامتداد المقرر
الامتداد (al-imtidād): الحد الزمني المقرر لكل عبادة حتى يسقط القضاء أو يجب
العارض الأصلي (al-‘āriḍ al-aṣlī): الجنون الذي يولَد به الإنسان ويستمر، وعلاجه يختلف عن العارض الحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the Hanafi scholars apply istihsan to temporary madness?
A) Because the qiyas was weak.
B) To avoid hardship (haraj).
C) Because it is similar to childhood.
D) Because the prophets are protected from it.

Key Takeaway
Madness does not negate capacity for obligation; short-term madness is pardoned by istihsan to avoid hardship, unlike the strict qiyas ruling.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Limits of Extension (Imtidad) for Madness in Acts of Worship
Analyzing the specific extension limits for madness in prayer, fasting, and zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the extension limit for madness in prayers, fasting, and zakat, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Extension limits vary by worship: for prayers, hours vs. six prayers; for fasting, the whole month; for zakat, most of the year suffices.

This passage details the specific temporal thresholds for madness to be considered 'extended' and thus requiring qada'. The imams differ: for prayers, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf use an increase in hours, while Muhammad uses exceeding a day and night (six prayers). Similar differences exist for zakat. For fasting, the limit is the entire month of Ramadan.

Guided Path
1
Note That Extension Limits Differ by Act of Worship
Read the passage from 'Wa hadd al-imtidad...' and observe that each worship has its own threshold.
Prayer, fasting, and zakat are discussed.
2
Understand the Two Positions for Prayers
Compare Muhammad's view (exceeding a day and night = six prayers) with Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf (increase in hours).
The key difference: counting prayers vs. considering time as a substitute.
3
Analyze the Ruling for Fasting
Note that the limit is covering the entire month of Ramadan; repetition (takrar) is not considered because a full month is already a unit.
No intra-Hanafi disagreement mentioned for fasting.
4
Examine the Disagreement for Zakat
Muhammad requires the full year; Abu Yusuf considers most of the year sufficient due to ease.
This mirrors the prayer disagreement in principle.

Chapter on Matters That Affect Legal Capacity. The impediments are of two types: heavenly and acquired. As for the heavenly, they are: childhood, madness, imbecility, forgetfulness, sleep, fainting, slavery, illness, menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and death. As for the acquired, they are two types: from oneself and from others. As for that from oneself: ignorance, intoxication, jest, foolishness, error, and travel. As for that from others: coercion with compulsion and without compulsion. As for madness: by analogy it cancels all acts of worship, because it contradicts capacity, so performance is nullified, and obligation is nullified due to its non-existence. However, they exercised istihsan in this case when it disappears before extension, so they made it a pardon, and attached it to sleep and fainting. That is because since it contradicts the capacity of performance, by analogy it is as we said—do you not see that the prophets (peace and blessings be upon them) were protected from it. But when it does not extend, it does not cause hardship, as we said. They differed on this: Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) said this is when it is an accidental impediment, to be attached to the impediments. But if a minor reaches puberty as a madman, then when it disappears, he becomes in the same meaning as a minor who reaches puberty. Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: he and the first case are equal, and he considers his condition in what he emerges from, and attaches it to his origin. And in the original constitution of creation, it varies between prolonged and short madness, so this origin is attached to the ruling that is not encompassed by the impediment, and that is in original madness when it disappears before the end of the month of Ramadan. The limit of extension differs according to acts of worship: As for prayers: according to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), it is by exceeding a day and a night, considering the prayers, so that they become six, thus entering the limit of repetition. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on them) considered time in place of the prayer for ease, so they considered increase in hours. As for fasting: by encompassing the month of Ramadan, and repetition is not considered because that is only established by a year. As for zakat: by encompassing the year according to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), and Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) considered most of the year in place of all of it in what extends, acting on ease and leniency. If it disappears before this limit and it is original, then this is according to this difference. And we have already explained before that madness does not contradict the capacity of obligation, because it does not contradict.
Source pages p. 723

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on impediments (al-‘awāriḍ) that affect legal capacity (al-ahliyya). The author classifies impediments into two main types: heavenly (samaawī) which are involuntary and beyond human control, and acquired (muktasab) which are voluntary or caused by human action. The heavenly impediments include childhood, madness, imbecility, forgetfulness, sleep, fainting, slavery, illness, menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and death. The acquired are subdivided into those from oneself (ignorance, intoxication, jest, foolishness, error, travel) and those from others (coercion with or without compulsion). The author then focuses on madness (junūn) as a heavenly impediment. By strict analogy (qiyās), madness would nullify all acts of worship because it negates the capacity for performance (ahliyya al-adā’), thereby nullifying performance and hence obligation. However, the Hanafi scholars applied istiḥsān (juristic preference) to treat temporary madness that disappears before extending beyond a certain limit as pardoned, likening it to sleep and fainting. This is because short-term madness, like sleep, does not cause excessive hardship. The scholars differed on the details: Abu Yusuf distinguished between accidental madness (original) and madness occurring in a minor who reaches puberty already mad, treating the latter like a minor attaining sanity. Muhammad considered both cases the same, linking the ruling to the origin of the madness. The text then explains that the limit of extension (imtidād) varies by act of worship. For prayers, according to Muhammad, the madness must extend beyond a day and a night so that six prayers are missed, which enters the criterion of repetition (takrār). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, for ease, consider increase in hours as the threshold. For fasting, the limit is covering the entire month of Ramadan; repetition is not a factor since a full month is already a unit. For zakāt, Muhammad requires the madness to cover the entire year (ḥawal), while Abu Yusuf considers most of the year sufficient for continuous obligations, again for ease. If the madness is original and disappears before these limits, the rulings differ. Finally, the author reiterates that madness does not negate the capacity for obligation (ahliyya al-wujūb), because it does not negate the subjecthood required for obligation—only performance is affected.

Key Terms
الامتداد (al-imtidād): الحد الزمني المقرر لكل عبادة حتى يسقط القضاء أو يجب
التكرار (al-takrār): مبدأ اعتبار تكرار العبادة (مثل ست صلوات) كحد للامتداد
التيسير (al-taysīr): مبدأ التخفيف الذي استند إليه أبو حنيفة وأبو يوسف في اعتبار الوقت مقام الصلاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the threshold for temporary madness to be pardoned for prayers according to Muhammad (Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani)?
A) Increase in hours beyond a single prayer.
B) Exceeding a day and a night (six prayers).
C) Covering the entire month of Ramadan.
D) Covering the entire year.

Key Takeaway
Extension limits for madness in worship vary: prayers have a short threshold (hours or six prayers), fasting covers the whole month, and zakat covers the full year or most of it.

Thresholds of Insanity Duration for Acts of Worship (Hadd al-Imtidad)
Detailed thresholds of insanity for three acts of worship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi thresholds of insanity duration for prayers, fasting, and zakat, and apply the principle of taysir to derive the ruling for a given duration.

Core Idea
Insanity duration thresholds differ per act: prayers use time substitution, fasting requires a full month, zakat requires a year or most of it.

In this lesson, we explore how the Hanafi school sets distinct minimum durations for insanity to affect the obligation of different acts of worship. The passage from al-Bazdawi's usul reveals an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether to count exact missed units or substitute time for ease. Understanding these thresholds is crucial for determining whether qada (make-up) is required for an insane person's missed acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three acts of worship
The passage explicitly discusses prayers, fasting, and zakat. Note that the threshold differs for each.
These are the primary ibadat addressed; other acts may follow different rules.
2
Analyze the threshold for prayers
Muhammad sets the limit at more than a day and a night so that six prayers are missed (the usual number in a day and night). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf substitute time for the number of prayers, measuring the excess in hours, applying the principle of taysir (ease).
This is a clear intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: counting exact prayers vs. time duration.
3
Analyze the threshold for fasting
The insanity must encompass the entire month of Ramadan. Repetition (tikrar) is not considered because a full year would be needed to establish repetition, and the month is already a complete unit.
No substitution here; the full month is the only standard.
4
Analyze the threshold for zakat
Muhammad requires the insanity to cover the entire year (hawl). Abu Yusuf substitutes most of the year for the whole year for what extends, again applying taysir and takhfif (leniency).
Only most of the year is needed according to Abu Yusuf; the actual duration of zakat obligation is a year.
5
Apply the rule to a hypothetical scenario
If insanity lasts 20 days (covering part of Ramadan and a few days outside), determine which obligations are affected using the thresholds from each imam.
This reinforces the practical difference between the positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحد الامتداد يختلف باختلاف الطاعات: فأما في الصلوات: فبأن يزيد على يوم وليلة باعتبار الصلوات عند محمد رحمه الله ؛ لتصير ستاً، فيدخل في حد التكرار. وأقام أبو حنيفة وأبو يوسف رحمهما الله الوقت فيه مقامَ الصلاة؛ تيسيراً، فاعتبرا الزيادة بالساعات. وفي الصوم: بأن يَستغرق شهر رمضان، ولم يُعتبر التكرار ؛ لأن ذلك لا يثبت إلا بحول. وفي الزكاة بأن يستغرق الحول عند محمد رحمه الله، وأقام أبو يوسف رحمه الله أكثر الحولِ مقامَ كله فيما يمتد؛ عملاً بالتيسير والتخفيف. فإذا زال قبل هذا الحد وهو أصلي : كان على هذا الاختلاف.
And the limit of duration differs according to the acts of worship: As for prayers: that it exceeds a day and a night, considering the prayers according to Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, so that they become six, and thus enter into the limit of repetition. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on them, substituted time for prayer in it; making it easy, so they considered the increase in hours. Regarding fasting: that it encompasses the entire month of Ramadan, and repetition was not considered because that is only established after a year. Regarding zakat: that it encompasses the entire year according to Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, substituted most of the year for the whole of it in what extends, acting on ease and leniency. So if it (insanity) ends before this limit and it is original, then it is according to this difference.
Source pages p. 724

Commentary
This passage explains how the minimum duration of insanity that triggers a change in obligation varies for different acts of worship. For prayers, Muhammad considers that the insanity must last more than a day and a night, so that the missed prayers become six, entering the realm of repetition. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, for ease, consider the duration in hours rather than the exact number of prayers. For fasting, the insanity must cover the entire month of Ramadan; repetition is not considered because a whole year is needed for that. For zakat, Muhammad requires the insanity to cover the entire year, while Abu Yusuf allows most of the year to suffice, again for ease. If the insanity ends before these thresholds, the ruling depends on whether the insanity is original or accidental, as per the earlier difference.

Key Terms
حد الامتداد (hadd al-imtidad): The minimum duration of insanity that affects the legal obligation for a specific act of worship.
تكرار (tikrar): Repetition; for prayers, missing more than one prayer (six) indicates a pattern; for fasting or zakat, repetition requires a full year.
تيسير (taysir): Ease; the principle used by Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf to substitute time for exact number of prayers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad, what is the minimum duration of insanity that affects the obligation of prayers?
A) More than a day and a night
B) More than six days
C) The entire month of Ramadan
D) Most of the year

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines distinct durations of insanity for different acts of worship, with internal differences grounded in the principle of taysir (ease).

Insanity Does Not Negate Capacity for Obligation: Hanafi Principles
Establishing that insanity does not negate capacity for obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why insanity does not negate ahliyyat al-wujub in Hanafi usul, and apply the distinction between capacity for obligation and capacity for performance to cases of inheritance, property damages, and faith.

Core Idea
Insanity preserves capacity for obligation but blocks performance; the dhimma remains intact.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul clarifies a fundamental principle: the insane person retains legal capacity to acquire rights and obligations (ahliyyat al-wujub) because insanity does not eliminate the dhimma. However, performance (ahliyyat al-ada') is restricted, except in certain rights of people. This distinction has important implications for inheritance, property liability, and even matters of faith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The passage begins by affirming that insanity does not negate ahliyyat al-wujub. Why? Because it does not negate the dhimma, nor the potential for reward in the afterlife.
2
Analyze the evidence (istidlal)
The author presents concrete examples: the insane inherits and owns property. These acts require capacity for obligation (to receive rights). Also, the insane is fully liable for property damages (daman), which shows capacity for obligation in rights of people.
3
Distinguish capacity types
Ahliyyat al-wujub is retained; ahliyyat al-ada' (performance) is negated due to lack of intellect. However, in huquq al-ibad, performance may be required through a guardian to prevent injustice.
4
Examine the exception for faith (iman)
The insane person's own profession of faith is invalid because its pillars (contract and performance) are missing. Yet, the insane can become a believer by following his parents. This shows that faith is legislated for him but his own act is not valid.
5
Compare with the minor (sighar)
A minor initially lacks intellect like an insane, but when reason is attained, partial capacity for performance exists. The minor's status as an excuse allows dropping some obligations, but not the obligatory status itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا من قبلُ أن الجنونَ لا ينافي أهلية الوجوب؛ لأنه لا ينافي الذمة، ولا ينافي حكم الواجب، وهو الثوابُ في الآخرة إذا احتمل الأداء، ألا يُرى أن المجنون يَرثُ، ويملك، وذلك ولايةٌ، إلا أن ينعدم الأداء، فيصير الوجوب عدماً بناء عليه. ولهذا قلنا: إن المجنون يؤاخذ بضمان الأفعال في الأموال على الكمال ؛ لأنه أهل لحكمه على ما قلنا فإذا ثبتت الأهلية كان هذا العارض من أسباب الحجر والحجر عن الأقوال عباراته. صحيح، ففسدت :وقلنا : لما لم يصح إيمانه ؛ لعدم ركنه ، وهو العقد، والأداء أيضاً، فلم يكن حَجْراً؛ لأن عدم الحكم لعدم الركن: ليس من باب الحجر، ولكن الإيمان مشروع في حقه، حتى صار مؤمناً تبعاً لأبويه. كذلك قال في الجامع»، فلم يصح التكليف بوجه إلا في حقوق العباد، فإن امرأة المجنون إذا أسلمت عُرض الإسلام على ولي المجنون؛ دفعاً للظلم بقدر الإمكان. وما كان ضرراً يحتمل السقوط: فغير مشروع في حقه. وما كان قبيحاً لا يحتمل العفو فثابتٌ في حقه، حتى إنه يصير مر مرتداً تبعاً لأبويه. والتمييز. وأما الصَّغَر في أول أحواله فمثلُ الجنون أيضاً؛ لأنه عديم العقل وأما إذا عَقَلَ : فقد أصاب ضرباً من أهلية الأداء، لكن الصبا عُذر مع ذلك، فسقط به ما يحتمل السقوط عن البالغ، فقلنا لا تسقط عنه فرضية
And we have previously explained that insanity does not negate capacity for obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub) because it does not negate the dhimma (legal personality), nor the ruling of the obligatory, which is reward in the Hereafter if it (the insane) can potentially perform. Do you not see that the insane person inherits, and owns property, and that is a form of guardianship (wilaya), except that performance is absent, so the obligation becomes non-existent based on that. For this reason we said: The insane person is held liable for damages (daman) from actions regarding property in full, because he is capable of its ruling as we said. So when capacity is established, this accidental condition is a cause of restriction (hajr), and restriction concerning statements (aqwal) his expressions (ibarat) are invalid. And we said: Since his faith (iman) was not valid due to the absence of its pillar, which is contract (aqd), and performance (ada') also, so it is not a restriction (hajr) because the absence of a ruling due to the absence of a pillar is not from the category of restriction. But faith is legislated for him, so that he becomes a believer following his parents. Likewise it is said in al-Jami'. So taklif (obligation) is not valid in any way except in the rights of people (huquq al-ibad). For instance, if the wife of an insane person converts to Islam, Islam is presented to the guardian of the insane person, to prevent injustice as much as possible. And what is harmful and can potentially fall away is not legislated for him. And what is inherently evil (qabih) that does not admit of pardon is established for him, to the extent that he becomes an apostate (murtadd) following his parents. And discrimination (tamyiz). As for being a minor (sighar) in its initial state, it is like insanity because he lacks intellect. But if he gains intellect (aqala), then he has attained a kind of capacity for performance (ahliyyat al-ada'), but being a minor is an excuse along with that, so what can potentially fall away from the adult falls away due to it. So we said: The obligatory status (fardiyya) does not fall away from him (the minor who has reason).
Source pages p. 724

Commentary
This passage establishes that insanity does not negate capacity for obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub) because it does not negate the dhimma (legal personality). The insane person can inherit and own property, indicating capacity for rights and obligations. However, performance (ada') is absent, so the actual obligation may be null. The insane person is fully liable for damages to property because he is capable of that ruling. Insanity is a cause of legal restriction (hajr), but this restriction does not apply to statements like faith; the insane person's profession of faith is invalid not due to restriction but due to missing pillars (contract and performance). Nevertheless, faith is legislated for him, so he can become a believer by following his parents. Obligation (taklif) is not valid for the insane except in matters of huquq al-ibad (rights of people), like if his wife converts, Islam is presented to his guardian to prevent injustice. Acts that are harmful but can be dropped are not legislated for him, but inherently evil acts that admit no pardon are established. The minor is like the insane initially, but when he gains reason, he has partial capacity for performance, and being a minor is an excuse that drops what can be dropped, but not the obligatory status (fardiyya) itself.

Key Terms
أهلية الوجوب (ahliyyat al-wujub): Legal capacity to acquire rights and obligations; it is not negated by insanity.
الذمة (al-dhimma): Legal personality or repository of rights and obligations; it remains intact despite insanity.
حجر (hajr): Legal restriction preventing the exercise of certain acts; insanity is a cause of hajr regarding statements (aqwal) and financial transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
