The Four Types of Worldly Rulings After Death
Classification of worldly rulings into four types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify worldly rulings (ahkam al-dunya) into four types and state the status of each for the deceased.

Core Idea
Worldly rulings are of four types; only the first (obligation) is lifted by death.

This lesson examines the classification of worldly rulings after death as presented in Usul al-Bazdawi. The author divides rulings into two realms: worldly and Hereafter. Worldly rulings are further split into four categories based on their purpose: obligation, prescribed for another's need, prescribed for one's own need, and those not suitable for need. Understanding this taxonomy clarifies which obligations cease and which rights persist.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two broad categories of rulings
The text first divides rulings into ahkam al-dunya (worldly) and ahkam al-akhirah (Hereafter). Focus on the worldly rulings.
2
List the four types of worldly rulings
The four types are: (1) matters of obligation (taklif), (2) prescribed for another's need, (3) prescribed for one's own need, (4) not suitable for fulfilling one's need.
The author uses 'القسم الأول' for the first type, then 'الثاني', then general 'منها' for the rest.
3
Determine the fate of each type upon death
For the first type (obligation), the address is lifted because its purpose is lost. For the second type (rights of others), the text later distinguishes between rights attached to assets and debts. For the third and fourth types, the passage does not elaborate but implies they may persist or be irrelevant.
The rest of the passage focuses on the second type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الموتُ : فإنه عجز كله، مناف لأهلية أحكام الدنيا مما فيه تكليف، حتى وضعت العبادات كلها عنه. والأحكام نوعان : أحكام الدنيا، وأحكام الآخرة. فأما أحكام الدنيا فأنواع أربعة: الأداء - القسم الأول - منها : ما هو من باب التكليف. والثاني : ما شرع عليه لحاجة غيره. ومنها : ما شرع له لحاجته. ومنها ما لا يصلح لقضاء حاجته، هذه أحكام الدنيا. فأما القسم الأول: فقد وضع عنه الخطاب؛ لفوت غرضه، وهو عن اختيار ولهذا قلنا إن الزكاة تبطل عنه، وكذلك سائر القرب، وإنما يبقى عليه المأثم. وأما القسم الثاني : فإنه إن كان حقاً متعلقاً بالعين: يبقى ببقائه؛ لأن فعله فيه غير مقصود. وإن كان ديناً: لم يبق بمجرد الذمة حتى يُضم إليه مال، أو ما تُؤكَّد به الذمم، وهو ذمة الكفيل؛ لأن ضعف الذمة بالموت فوق الضعف بالرق؛ لأن الرق يرجى زواله غالباً، وهذا لا يُرجى زواله غالباً، فقلنا إنها لا تحتمل الدين بنفسها. ولهذا قيل : إن الكفالة عن الميت المفلس لا تصح، وهو قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله، كأن الدين ساقط؛ لأن ثبوته بالمطالبة، والكفالة لالتزام المطالبة، وقد عُدمت. بخلاف العبد المحجور يُقِرُّ بالدين، فتكفل عنه رجل: صح؛ لأن
As for death: it is total incapacity, contradictory to the capacity for worldly rulings that involve obligation, until all acts of worship are dropped from him. And rulings are of two types: rulings of this world and rulings of the Hereafter. As for rulings of this world, they are four types: (1) Performance: the first type: that which falls under obligation. The second: that which is prescribed for the sake of another's need. And among them: that which is prescribed for his own need. And among them: that which is not suitable to fulfill his need – these are the rulings of this world. As for the first type: the address is lifted from him because its purpose is lost, and it is not by choice. Therefore we say that zakat is nullified from him, as well as all other acts of devotion, and only sin remains upon him. As for the second type: if it is a right attached to a physical asset, it remains as long as the asset remains, because his action is not intended in it. And if it is a debt, it does not remain merely by the dhimmah (obligation) until wealth is added to it, or what strengthens the dhimmah, i.e., the guarantee of a surety; because the weakness of the dhimmah due to death is greater than weakness due to slavery; because slavery is usually hoped to be removed, while this is not usually hoped to be removed. So we say that the dhimmah alone does not bear the debt. For this reason it is said that suretyship (kafalah) for a deceased insolvent is not valid – this is the saying of Abu Hanifah, may Allah have mercy on him – as if the debt is dropped because its establishment is through demand, and suretyship is to undertake the demand, but it is absent. Contrary to a slave under interdiction who confesses a debt, then a man becomes his surety: it is valid because
Source pages p. 737

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi discusses the effect of death on legal capacity (ahliyyah). Death is defined as total incapacity, negating the capacity for worldly rulings that involve obligation (taklif). Consequently, all acts of worship fall away from the deceased. The author divides rulings into two broad categories: those of this world (dunya) and those of the Hereafter (akhirah). Rulings of this world are further subdivided into four types: (1) matters of obligation (taklif), (2) those prescribed for the benefit of others, (3) those prescribed for one's own benefit, and (4) those that are not suitable for fulfilling one's need. For the deceased, the first type (obligations) is lifted because the purpose (reward/punishment) is no longer achievable. Thus, obligations like zakat are nullified, and only sin from missed obligations remains. The second type includes rights of others: if it is a right attached to a physical asset (e.g., collateral), it remains with the asset. If it is a debt, it does not persist solely through the dhimmah (personal obligation) unless accompanied by actual wealth or a guarantor (kafalah). This is because the dhimmah of the deceased is weaker than that of a slave (who may become free). Therefore, a debt without assets or surety is considered dropped, as it requires the possibility of demand. Hence, suretyship for a deceased insolvent is invalid according to Abu Hanifah. This is contrasted with a slave under interdiction (mahjur) who confesses a debt—suretyship for him is valid, because his dhimmah is still intact and can be strengthened. The passage ends mid-sentence, continuing the comparison.

Key Terms
التكليف (al-taklif): The address of the Lawgiver demanding an action from the legally competent person.
الذمة (al-dhimmah): A legal vessel or capacity that bears obligations and rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the classification, which type of worldly ruling is the first type (القسم الأول)?
A) Matters prescribed for another's need.
B) Matters of obligation (taklif).
C) Matters prescribed for one's own need.
D) Matters not suitable for one's need.

Key Takeaway
Worldly rulings are classified into four types; only the first type (obligation) is lifted by death, while others may persist under conditions.

Debts and Suretyship After Death: Hanafi Ruling
Effect of death on debts and suretyship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the persistence of debts after death and explain why suretyship for a deceased insolvent is invalid.

Core Idea
Debts do not persist solely by dhimmah after death; suretyship for a deceased insolvent is invalid because the demand is absent.

This lesson delves into the second type of worldly rulings – matters prescribed for another's need. The text distinguishes between rights attached to physical assets and unsecured debts. The author explains that the deceased's dhimmah is too weak to bear a debt alone, analogizing it to the weakness of a slave's dhimmah. Consequently, suretyship for a deceased insolvent is invalid, as the debt is considered dropped due to the impossibility of demand.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between rights attached to assets and unsecured debts
If the right is attached to a physical asset (e.g., collateral), it remains with the asset because the deceased's action is not needed. If it is an unsecured debt, it does not persist by the dhimmah alone.
2
Understand the weakness of the deceased's dhimmah
The dhimmah of the deceased is weaker than that of a slave because slavery may end, but death is permanent. Therefore, the dhimmah alone cannot sustain a debt without wealth or a guarantor.
This is a talil (causal reasoning) based on hopelessness of recovery.
3
Apply the ruling on suretyship
Suretyship (kafalah) for a deceased insolvent is invalid according to Abu Hanifah because the debt's existence depends on the ability to demand payment, which is absent after death. The surety would be undertaking something non-existent.
Contrast with a slave under interdiction: his dhimmah is intact so suretyship is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): Suretyship; a contract whereby a person undertakes to fulfill an obligation if the principal fails.
المفلس (al-muflis): An insolvent person who has no assets to pay debts.
الذمة (al-dhimmah): A legal vessel or capacity that bears obligations and rights.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Four Categories of Worldly Rulings for the Deceased in Hanafi Usul
Classification and explanation of the four categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the four categories of worldly rulings that apply to a deceased person according to Hanafi usul, and state which category is entirely lifted and why.

Core Idea
Taklif (worship obligations) is lifted; debts require property or guarantor; property rights and personal needs persist.

When a person dies, not all legal rulings are cancelled. The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between different types of rulings: some are completely lifted (taklif), some persist in a weakened form (debts), some remain attached to specific property, and some continue to serve the deceased's need. This lesson unpacks that classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions of rulings
First, note that rulings are divided into those of this world (ahkam al-dunya) and the Hereafter (ahkam al-akhirah). Only the former are discussed here.
2
Distinguish the four subtypes of worldly rulings
The passage lists four: (a) taklif (obligatory acts of worship), (b) rights of others – attached to specific property ('ayn) or as a debt (dayn), (c) rights prescribed for the deceased's own need (funeral expenses, debts, bequests), and (d) mamlukiyyah (property/slavery).
3
Determine the survival of each subtype
For taklif: completely lifted because the purpose (choice and ability) is lost. For 'ayn: remains by the survival of the property. For dayn: does not remain by the dhimma alone; requires property or a guarantor. For personal needs: remains because death does not eliminate the need. For mamlukiyyah: it is subordinate.
4
Understand the reasoning for debts
Debts do not survive in the dhimma alone because the dhimma after death is weaker than that of a slave; slavery might end, but death is permanent. Therefore, the debt needs external support (property or guarantor) to be enforceable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تكليف، حتى وضعت العبادات كلها عنه.
والأحكام نوعان : أحكام الدنيا، وأحكام الآخرة.
فأما أحكام الدنيا فأنواع أربعة:
-١ - قسم منها : ما هو من باب التكليف.
والثاني : ما شرع عليه لحاجة غيره.
ومنها : ما شرع له لحاجته.
٤- ومنها ما لا يصلح لقضاء حاجته، هذه أحكام الدنيا.
فأما القسم الأول: فقد وضع عنه الخطاب؛ لفَوْت غرضه، وهو عن اختيار ولهذا قلنا إن الزكاة تبطل عنه، وكذلك سائر القُرب، وإنما يبقى عليه المأثم.
وأما القسم الثاني : فإنه إن كان حقاً متعلقاً بالعين: يبقى ببقائه ؛ لأن فعله فيه غير مقصود.
وإن كان ديناً: لم يبق بمجرد الذمة حتى يُضم إليه مال، أو ما تُؤكَّد به الذمم، وهو ذمة الكفيل ؛ لأن ضعف الذمة بالموت فوق الضعف بالرق؛ لأن الرق يرجى زواله غالباً، وهذا لا يُرجى زواله غالباً، فقلنا إنها لا تحتمل الدين بنفسها.

بخلاف العبد المحجور يُقِرُّ بالدين، فتكفل عنه رجل: صح؛ لأن ذمته في حقه كاملة، وإنما ضمّت المالية إليها في حق المولى. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله : صح؛ لأن الدين مطالب به، ولكنا عجزنا عنها. والجواب عنه: أنه غيرُ مطالب به؛ لأن ذلك انعدم لمعنى في محل الدين، لا لعجزنا لمعنى فينا، فلهذا لزمته الديون مضافاً إلى سبب صح في حياته ، ولهذا صح الضمان عنه إذا خلف مالاً ، أو كفيلاً.
الثلث.
وإن كان شرع عليه بطريق الصلة بطل، إلا أن يوصي: فيصح من الثلث.
وأما الذي شُرع له : فبناء على حاجته ؛ لأن مرافق البشر إنما شرعت لهم لحاجتهم ؛ لأن العبودية لازمة للبشر، والموتُ لا ينافي الحاجة، فبقي له ما تنقضي به الحاجة ولذلك بقيت التركةُ على حكم ملكه عند قيام الديون عليه، لذلك قُدَّم جهازه ، ثم ديونه، ولذلك صحت وصاياه كلها واقعةً ومفوضة.
ولذلك بقيت الكتابة بعد موت المولى، وبعد موت المكاتب عن وفاء؛ لأن المكاتب مالك لحكم عقد الكتابة، وهي مشروعة لحاجة المكاتب، وهي أقوى الحوائج ، ألا يُرى أنه نُدب فيه حط بعض البدل، فإذا جاز بقاء مالكية المولى بعد موته ليصير معتقاً : فلأن تبقى هذه المالكية ليصير معتقاً أولى.

Taklif, such that all acts of worship are lifted from him. And the rulings are two types: rulings of this world and rulings of the Hereafter. As for rulings of this world, they are four types: 1. A section: that which is from the category of taklif. 2. A second: that which is prescribed upon him for the need of another. 3. Among them: that which is prescribed for him for his own need. 4. Among them: that which is not suitable for fulfilling his need. These are the rulings of this world. As for the first section: the address has been lifted from him due to the loss of its purpose—it is without choice—and for this we said that zakah becomes invalid for him, and likewise all other acts of nearness (qurb), and only the sin remains upon him. As for the second section: if it is a right attached to the 'ayn (property), it remains by its survival because his action concerning it is not intended. And if it is a debt, it does not remain by the mere dhimma (legal liability) until property is added to it, or something that strengthens dhimmah—which is the dhimma of the guarantor—because the weakness of the dhimma due to death is greater than weakness due to slavery; because slavery is usually hoped to be removed, but this is not usually hoped to be removed. So we said that it (the dhimma) does not bear the debt by itself. For this reason it is said: guarantee (kafalah) for a deceased insolvent is not valid—this is the saying of Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him—as if the debt is dropped because its establishment is through demand, and guarantee is for undertaking demand, which has been lost. Contrary to a legally restricted slave who admits to a debt, then a man guarantees for him: it is valid because his dhimma is complete in his own right, and financial capacity is only attached to it in relation to the master. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, said: it is valid because the debt is demanded, but we are unable (to demand) from him. The answer to that: he is not demanded because that (demand) is non-existent due to a meaning in the locus of the debt, not due to our inability for a meaning in us. For this reason, debts become binding upon him added to a cause that was valid during his lifetime, and for this reason guarantee (daman) is valid for him if he leaves property or a guarantor. The third. And if it was prescribed upon him by way of connection (silah), it becomes invalid unless he bequeaths: then it is valid from the third. As for that which is prescribed for him: it is based on his need because the amenities of humans were only prescribed for them due to their need, since servitude is necessary for humans, and death does not negate need. So what fulfills the need remains for him. Therefore, the estate remains under the ruling of his ownership when there are debts upon him. For that reason, his shroud is given precedence, then his debts, and for that reason all his bequests are valid, whether executed or delegated. Likewise, the contract of kitabah (manumission contract) remains after the death of the master, and after the death of the mukatib with ability to pay, because the mukatib is owner of the contract of kitabah, which is legislated for the need of the mukatib, and it is the strongest of needs. Do you not see that a reduction of some of the price is recommended in it? So if it is permissible for the master's ownership to remain after his death so that he becomes free, then for this ownership to remain so that he becomes free is more appropriate. As for slavery (mamlukiyyah), it is subordinate in this chapter. For this reason, inheritance is established by way of succession (khilafah) from the deceased, looking out for him from one aspect, until it is given to those connected to him by lineage, or by cause and debt, or by debt without lineage or cause.
Source pages p. 737

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh explains how different types of rulings (ahkam) apply to a deceased person. The author first distinguishes between rulings of this world and the Hereafter, then divides worldly rulings into four categories. The first category is taklif (obligatory acts of worship). For the deceased, this is entirely lifted because the purpose of taklif—choice and ability—is lost. So acts like zakah are invalid if performed after death, and only the sin remains if they were missed. The second category is rights of others. If the right is attached to a specific property ('ayn), it remains because the deceased's action is not needed. If it is a debt, it does not remain in the dhimma alone after death; it requires property or a guarantor (kafeel) because the dhimma after death is weaker than that of a slave. This leads to a dispute: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, kafalah for a deceased insolvent is invalid because the debt is no longer demandable; but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) argue it is valid because the debt is still demandable in principle, even if we are unable to collect. The author defends Abu Hanifa's view by noting that the inability to demand is due to a defect in the debt's locus, not in us. The third category is rights prescribed for the deceased's own need, such as funeral expenses, debts, and bequests. These remain because death does not eliminate the need for these amenities. Thus the estate is treated as still belonging to the deceased for these purposes. The fourth category is mamlukiyyah (slavery or property relationships), which is subordinate. Inheritance exists as a form of succession to the deceased, distributing his wealth to his heirs.

Key Terms
تكليف (taklif): Address (khitab) that obligates an action, requiring choice and ability; acts of worship like salah, zakah
ذمة (dhimma): Legal liability or capacity to bear obligations; after death it is weak and cannot hold debts alone without property or guarantor
كفالة (kafalah): Guarantee to undertake demand for a debt; for deceased insolvent, it is invalid according to Abu Hanifa because the debt's demandability is lost

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is taklif (obligatory worship) lifted from the deceased?
A) Because death cancels all rights
B) Because the purpose of taklif (choice and ability) is lost
C) Because the deceased no longer needs reward
D) Because debts take priority

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, death lifts only taklif; property rights and debts persist under specific conditions.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Hanafi Dispute on Kafalah for a Deceased Insolvent
Detailed discussion of the intra-Hanafi dispute on kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the differing positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the validity of kafalah for a deceased insolvent, and defend the Hanafi position using the reasoning of dhimma weakness and demandability.

Core Idea
Kafalah requires a demandable debt; death renders the debt non-demandable, so kafalah is invalid according to Abu Hanifa.

One of the key applications of the principle that debts do not survive in the dhimma alone is the dispute over kafalah for a deceased insolvent. Imam Abu Hanifa considers it invalid, while his students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, argue it remains valid. This lesson unpacks the proof and counter-proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The issue is the validity of kafalah (guarantee) for the debt of a deceased insolvent – i.e., a person who died with debts and no property.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds the guarantee is invalid. His proof: the debt is established through demandability (mutalabah). After death, demandability is lost because the dhimma cannot sustain it. Kafalah is an undertaking to meet the demand; without demand, it is void.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold it is valid. Their proof: the debt is still owed in principle; our inability to collect is a practical hindrance, not a legal nullity. The debt remains demandable.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The response distinguishes between inability due to a defect in the locus (the deceased's dhimma) and inability due to the creditor. The debt's demand is legally non-existent because the cause (the dhimma) is too weak, not because we are unable to act.
5
Apply the same principle to other cases
Contrast with the case of a slave: his dhimma is complete, so kafalah for his debt is valid. Also, if the deceased left property or a guarantor, kafalah becomes valid because the dhimma is strengthened.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تكليف، حتى وضعت العبادات كلها عنه.
والأحكام نوعان : أحكام الدنيا، وأحكام الآخرة.
فأما أحكام الدنيا فأنواع أربعة:
-١ - قسم منها : ما هو من باب التكليف.
والثاني : ما شرع عليه لحاجة غيره.
ومنها : ما شرع له لحاجته.
٤- ومنها ما لا يصلح لقضاء حاجته، هذه أحكام الدنيا.
فأما القسم الأول: فقد وضع عنه الخطاب؛ لفَوْت غرضه، وهو عن اختيار ولهذا قلنا إن الزكاة تبطل عنه، وكذلك سائر القُرب، وإنما يبقى عليه المأثم.
وأما القسم الثاني : فإنه إن كان حقاً متعلقاً بالعين: يبقى ببقائه ؛ لأن فعله فيه غير مقصود.
وإن كان ديناً: لم يبق بمجرد الذمة حتى يُضم إليه مال، أو ما تُؤكَّد به الذمم، وهو ذمة الكفيل ؛ لأن ضعف الذمة بالموت فوق الضعف بالرق؛ لأن الرق يرجى زواله غالباً، وهذا لا يُرجى زواله غالباً، فقلنا إنها لا تحتمل الدين بنفسها.
ولهذا قيل : إن الكفالة عن الميت المفلس لا تصح، وهو قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله ، كأن الدين ساقط ؛ لأن ثبوته بالمطالبة، والكفالة لالتزام المطالبة، وقد عُدمت.
بخلاف العبد المحجور يُقِرُّ بالدين، فتكفل عنه رجل: صح؛ لأن ذمته في حقه كاملة، وإنما ضمّت المالية إليها في حق المولى. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله : صح؛ لأن الدين مطالب به، ولكنا عجزنا عنها. والجواب عنه: أنه غيرُ مطالب به؛ لأن ذلك انعدم لمعنى في محل الدين، لا لعجزنا لمعنى فينا، فلهذا لزمته الديون مضافاً إلى سبب صح في حياته ، ولهذا صح الضمان عنه إذا خلف مالاً ، أو كفيلاً.
الثلث.
وإن كان شرع عليه بطريق الصلة بطل، إلا أن يوصي: فيصح من الثلث.
وأما الذي شُرع له : فبناء على حاجته ؛ لأن مرافق البشر إنما شرعت لهم لحاجتهم ؛ لأن العبودية لازمة للبشر، والموتُ لا ينافي الحاجة، فبقي له ما تنقضي به الحاجة ولذلك بقيت التركةُ على حكم ملكه عند قيام الديون عليه، لذلك قُدَّم جهازه ، ثم ديونه، ولذلك صحت وصاياه كلها واقعةً ومفوضة.
ولذلك بقيت الكتابة بعد موت المولى، وبعد موت المكاتب عن وفاء؛ لأن المكاتب مالك لحكم عقد الكتابة، وهي مشروعة لحاجة المكاتب، وهي أقوى الحوائج ، ألا يُرى أنه نُدب فيه حط بعض البدل، فإذا جاز بقاء مالكية المولى بعد موته ليصير معتقاً : فلأن تبقى هذه المالكية ليصير معتقاً أولى.
وأما المملوكية فهي تابعةٌ في الباب، ولهذا وجبت المواريث بطريق الخلافة عن الميت؛ نظراً له من وجه حتى صُرفت إلى من يتصل به نسباً ، أو سبباً وديناً ، أو ديناً بلا نسب وسبب.
Taklif, such that all acts of worship are lifted from him. And the rulings are two types: rulings of this world and rulings of the Hereafter. As for rulings of this world, they are four types: 1. A section: that which is from the category of taklif. 2. A second: that which is prescribed upon him for the need of another. 3. Among them: that which is prescribed for him for his own need. 4. Among them: that which is not suitable for fulfilling his need. These are the rulings of this world. As for the first section: the address has been lifted from him due to the loss of its purpose—it is without choice—and for this we said that zakah becomes invalid for him, and likewise all other acts of nearness (qurb), and only the sin remains upon him. As for the second section: if it is a right attached to the 'ayn (property), it remains by its survival because his action concerning it is not intended. And if it is a debt, it does not remain by the mere dhimma (legal liability) until property is added to it, or something that strengthens dhimmah—which is the dhimma of the guarantor—because the weakness of the dhimma due to death is greater than weakness due to slavery; because slavery is usually hoped to be removed, but this is not usually hoped to be removed. So we said that it (the dhimma) does not bear the debt by itself. For this reason it is said: guarantee (kafalah) for a deceased insolvent is not valid—this is the saying of Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him—as if the debt is dropped because its establishment is through demand, and guarantee is for undertaking demand, which has been lost. Contrary to a legally restricted slave who admits to a debt, then a man guarantees for him: it is valid because his dhimma is complete in his own right, and financial capacity is only attached to it in relation to the master. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, said: it is valid because the debt is demanded, but we are unable (to demand) from him. The answer to that: he is not demanded because that (demand) is non-existent due to a meaning in the locus of the debt, not due to our inability for a meaning in us. For this reason, debts become binding upon him added to a cause that was valid during his lifetime, and for this reason guarantee (daman) is valid for him if he leaves property or a guarantor. The third. And if it was prescribed upon him by way of connection (silah), it becomes invalid unless he bequeaths: then it is valid from the third. As for that which is prescribed for him: it is based on his need because the amenities of humans were only prescribed for them due to their need, since servitude is necessary for humans, and death does not negate need. So what fulfills the need remains for him. Therefore, the estate remains under the ruling of his ownership when there are debts upon him. For that reason, his shroud is given precedence, then his debts, and for that reason all his bequests are valid, whether executed or delegated. Likewise, the contract of kitabah (manumission contract) remains after the death of the master, and after the death of the mukatib with ability to pay, because the mukatib is owner of the contract of kitabah, which is legislated for the need of the mukatib, and it is the strongest of needs. Do you not see that a reduction of some of the price is recommended in it? So if it is permissible for the master's ownership to remain after his death so that he becomes free, then for this ownership to remain so that he becomes free is more appropriate. As for slavery (mamlukiyyah), it is subordinate in this chapter. For this reason, inheritance is established by way of succession (khilafah) from the deceased, looking out for him from one aspect, until it is given to those connected to him by lineage, or by cause and debt, or by debt without lineage or cause.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh explains how different types of rulings (ahkam) apply to a deceased person. The author first distinguishes between rulings of this world and the Hereafter, then divides worldly rulings into four categories. The first category is taklif (obligatory acts of worship). For the deceased, this is entirely lifted because the purpose of taklif—choice and ability—is lost. So acts like zakah are invalid if performed after death, and only the sin remains if they were missed. The second category is rights of others. If the right is attached to a specific property ('ayn), it remains because the deceased's action is not needed. If it is a debt, it does not remain in the dhimma alone after death; it requires property or a guarantor (kafeel) because the dhimma after death is weaker than that of a slave. This leads to a dispute: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, kafalah for a deceased insolvent is invalid because the debt is no longer demandable; but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) argue it is valid because the debt is still demandable in principle, even if we are unable to collect. The author defends Abu Hanifa's view by noting that the inability to demand is due to a defect in the debt's locus, not in us. The third category is rights prescribed for the deceased's own need, such as funeral expenses, debts, and bequests. These remain because death does not eliminate the need for these amenities. Thus the estate is treated as still belonging to the deceased for these purposes. The fourth category is mamlukiyyah (slavery or property relationships), which is subordinate. Inheritance exists as a form of succession to the deceased, distributing his wealth to his heirs.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Guarantee to undertake demand for a debt; for deceased insolvent, it is invalid according to Abu Hanifa because the debt's demandability is lost
ذمة (dhimma): Legal liability or capacity to bear obligations; after death it is weak and cannot hold debts alone without property or guarantor
مطالبة (mutalabah): Demandability; the right of the creditor to demand payment. It is the basis for the debt's enforceability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis of Abu Hanifa's ruling that kafalah for a deceased insolvent is invalid?
A) The debt is not owed.
B) The debt's demandability is lost.
C) The creditor cannot claim.
D) The deceased's heirs object.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa holds that kafalah for a deceased insolvent is invalid because demandability of the debt is lost; the Sahibayn disagree, but the relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's.

Khilafa: The Principle of Legal Succession After Death
Defining khilafa and distinguishing ta'liq bil-mawt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of khilafa and explain how ta'liq bil-mawt differs from other forms of suspension in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Death is a cause of khilafa, making rights effective immediately via substitution.

In this passage, al-Bazdawi introduces the concept of khilafa, the legal substitution that transfers or preserves rights after death. He contrasts ta'liq bil-mawt with other suspensions because death is certain and creates an immediate, deferred effect. Understanding this principle is essential for grasping how obligations like debts and contracts survive the deceased.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Find the term 'khilafa' in the Arabic text and note its definition as 'succession' from the deceased. The passage states that inheritances are established by way of khilafa.
Khilafa literally means 'substitution' or 'succession'.
2
Recognize the cause (sabab)
Identify death as the cause of khilafa. The text says 'al-mawt min asbab al-khilafa' (death is among the causes of succession).
Death is a certainty, making it a unique sabab.
3
Understand the effect
The effect is that a right is established immediately by way of succession, though its full realization may be deferred (e.g., after death). The text: 'yūjib ḥaqqan li-l-ḥāl bi-ṭarīq al-khilāfa ʿanhu'.
The right is effective now but fulfilled later.
4
Contrast with other suspensions
Contrast ta'liq bil-mawt with other forms of ta'liq (suspension). Other suspensions depend on uncertain future events and do not create an immediate right. Death is certain, so the right vests immediately via khilafa.
This uniqueness supports the continuity of certain contracts after death.
5
Apply to inheritance
Apply the principle to inheritance: the deceased's ownership of property is transferred to heirs immediately upon death via khilafa, because the right to inherit is tied to a need that persists (the need of the heirs).
Inheritance is a primary example of khilafa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذمته في حقه كاملة، وإنما ضمّت المالية إليها في حق المولى. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله : صح؛ لأن الدين مطالب به، ولكنا عجزنا عنها. والجواب عنه: أنه غيرُ مطالب به؛ لأن ذلك انعدم لمعنى في محل الدين، لا لعجزنا لمعنى فينا، فلهذا لزمته الديون مضافاً إلى سبب صح في حياته ، ولهذا صح الضمان عنه إذا خلف مالاً ، أو كفيلاً.
الثلث.
وإن كان شرع عليه بطريق الصلة بطل، إلا أن يوصي: فيصح من وأما الذي شُرع له : فبناء على حاجته؛ لأن مرافق البشر إنما شرعت لهم لحاجتهم؛ لأن العبودية لازمة للبشر، والموتُ لا ينافي الحاجة، فبقي له ما تنقضي به الحاجة ولذلك بقيت التركةُ على حكم ملكه عند قيام الديون عليه، لذلك قُدَّم جهازه ، ثم ديونه، ولذلك صحت وصاياه كلها واقعةً ومفوضة.
ولذلك بقيت الكتابة بعد موت المولى، وبعد موت المكاتب عن وفاء؛ لأن المكاتب مالك لحكم عقد الكتابة، وهي مشروعة لحاجة المكاتب، وهي أقوى الحوائج ، ألا يُرى أنه نُدب فيه حط بعض البدل، فإذا جاز بقاء مالكية المولى بعد موته ليصير معتقاً : فلأن تبقى هذه المالكية ليصير معتقاً أولى.

وأما المملوكية فهي تابعةٌ في الباب، ولهذا وجبت المواريث بطريق الخلافة عن الميت؛ نظراً له من وجه حتى صُرفت إلى من يتصل به نسباً ، أو سبباً وديناً ، أو ديناً بلا نسب وسبب.
ولهذا صار التعليق بالموت يخالف سائر وجوه التعليق؛ لأن الموت من أسباب الخلافة، فيصير التعليق به، وهو كائن بيقين إيجاب حق للحال بطريق الخلافة عنه.
ألا يُرى أن الخلافة إذا ثبت سببها ، وهو مرض الموت للوارث : ثبت به حق يصير به المريض محجوراً ، فكذلك إذا ثبت بالنص، وصار المال من ثمراته، فيُنظر من بَعد: فإن كان الحقُّ لازماً بأصله، مثل حق العتق بالتدبير : مَنَعَ الاعتراض عليه من المولى ؛ للزومه في نفسه، وللزومه في سببه، وهو معنى التعليق.
فلذلك بطل بيع المدبر، وصار ذلك كأم الولد، فإنها استحقت شيئين : حق العتق؛ لما بينا، وسقوط التقوم عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى؛ لأن التقوم بالإحراز يكون، وقد ذهب ؛ لأن الأمة في الأصل تُحرَزُ لماليتها، والمتعة منها ،تابعةٌ فإذا صارت فراشاً: صارت محصنةً محرزةً للمتعة، والمالية تابعةٌ فصار الإحراز عدماً في حق المالية، فلذلك ذهب التقوم ، وهو عِزَّة المالية، وانتسخت بعِزَّة المتعة، فتعدى الحكم الأول إلى المدبر ؛ لوجود معناه، دون الثاني.
ولهذا قلنا : إن المرأة تُغسل زوجها بعد الموت في عدتها؛ لأن الزوج مالك، فيبقى ملكه إلى انقضاء العدة فيما هو من حوائجه خاصةً بعد الموت.
بخلاف المرأة إذا ماتت ؛ لأنها مملوكة، وقد بطلت أهلية المملوكية، فلا تبقى حقاً لها؛ لأن ذلك حق عليها ، ألا يُرى أنه لا عدة عليه بعدها، ولو بقي ضرب من الملك لوجب مراعاته بالعدة؛ لأن ملك النكاح لم
His liability is complete in his own right, but financial liability is added to it in the right of the master. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said: It is valid because the debt is claimable, but we are incapable of it. The response to it is: He is not claimable because that deficiency is due to a meaning in the locus of the debt, not due to our incapacity because of a meaning in us. Therefore, debts are binding on him in addition to a cause valid during his life, and therefore the guarantee on his behalf is valid if he leaves property or a guarantor. The third. And if it is legislated by way of gift, it becomes void, unless he bequeaths: then it is valid from... And as for that which is legislated for him: it is based on his need, because human facilities are only legislated for them due to their need, because servitude is inherent to humans, and death does not negate need. So what fulfills the need remains for him. Therefore the estate remains under the rule of his ownership while debts are upon him. Therefore his shroud is prioritized, then his debts. Therefore all his bequests, whether absolute or delegated, are valid. Therefore the kitaba contract remains after the death of the master and after the death of the mukatib without payment, because the mukatib is owner of the ruling of the kitaba contract, and it is legislated for the need of the mukatib, and it is the strongest of needs. Do you not see that it is recommended to reduce part of the payment? So if the master's ownership remaining after his death to effect freedom is permissible, then it is more fitting for this ownership to remain to effect freedom. As for being owned, it is subsidiary in this chapter. Therefore inheritances are established by way of succession from the deceased, considering him in one aspect, so it is directed to those connected to him by lineage or cause and debt, or debt without lineage or cause. Therefore suspension upon death differs from other forms of suspension, because death is among the causes of succession, so suspension upon it, and it is certain to occur, establishes a right immediately by way of succession from him. Do you not see that when the cause of succession is established, which is the death-illness of the heir, a right is established by which the sick person becomes interdicted? So too when it is established by text, and the property becomes of its fruits. Then it is considered afterwards: if the right is binding in its origin, like the right of freedom by tadbir, it prevents objection from the master, due to its binding in itself and due to its binding in its cause, which is the meaning of suspension. Therefore the sale of the mudabbar is void, and it becomes like an umm walad. She deserves two things: the right of freedom, as we explained, and the dropping of marketability according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah Almighty have mercy on him), because marketability occurs by possession, and it has gone, because the slave-woman is originally possessed for her financial value, and the enjoyment from her is subsidiary. When she becomes a child-bearing concubine, she becomes protected and possessed for enjoyment, and the financial value becomes subsidiary. So possession becomes non-existent in respect of financial value. Therefore marketability, which is the dignity of financial value, goes away, and it is changed to the dignity of enjoyment. So the first ruling applies to the mudabbar due to the presence of its meaning, but not the second. Therefore we say: A woman may wash her husband after death during her waiting period, because the husband is an owner, so his ownership remains until the end of the waiting period in matters that are of his needs especially after death. Unlike the case when the woman dies, because she is owned, and the capacity of being owned has ceased, so no right remains for her, because that is a right upon her. Do you not see that there is no waiting period for him after her? And if some type of ownership remained, it would have to be observed by the waiting period, because the ownership of marriage did not...
Source pages p. 738

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh addresses the survival of certain legal relations after death, a key principle in Hanafi law. The text begins by discussing the liability of a deceased person's estate for debts, with a dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that the estate is liable because the debt is tied to a cause valid during life, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider the guarantee valid if the deceased left property or a guarantor. The author then explains that bequests are valid because they fulfill a need that persists after death, unlike gifts which are void unless bequeathed. The estate remains under the deceased's ownership for fulfilling needs like shroud and debts. The kitaba contract (contract of manumission by payment) continues after the master's death because the mukatib's need for freedom is paramount. The principle of 'khilafa' (succession) is introduced: death creates a substitution where rights and obligations transfer to heirs or continue for the deceased's benefit. Suspension upon death (ta'liq bil-mawt) differs from other suspensions because death is a certainty and creates an immediate effect via succession. This is illustrated by tadbir (declaration of freedom after death): the mudabbar cannot be sold because the right to freedom is already binding in its cause (the declaration) and in its origin. The case of umm walad (bondwoman who bears her master's child) is compared: she gains the right to freedom and the loss of marketability according to Abu Hanifa, because her possession shifts from financial value to enjoyment. This ruling extends to the mudabbar only for the freedom right, not the loss of marketability. Finally, the difference between a husband and wife upon death is noted: a husband's ownership over his wife's waiting period needs persists, so she can wash him, but a wife's death ends her capacity to be owned, so no reciprocal right exists.

Key Terms
خلافة (khilāfa): The legal substitution by which the rights and obligations of the deceased are transferred to heirs or maintained for the deceased's benefit after death.
تعليق بالموت (ta‘līq bi l-mawt): Suspension of a legal effect upon death, which creates an immediate right of succession, unlike other suspensions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does ta'liq bil-mawt differ from other forms of suspension?
A) Because it is the only suspension that creates a right.
B) Because death is a cause of khilafa and is certain, so the right is established immediately.
C) Because other suspensions require the consent of both parties.
D) Because the deceased's estate must first be inventoried.

Key Takeaway
Khilafa is the Hanafi principle that certain rights and obligations survive death through legal substitution, with ta'liq bil-mawt being uniquely immediate due to death's certainty.

Classification of Ignorance in Hanafi Usul
Defining four types of ignorance and their legal effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the four types of ignorance according to Hanafi usul, and state the legal effect (shubha, 'udhr, or no excuse) of each type.

Core Idea
Not all ignorance is equal: obstinate denial is no excuse, while genuine doubt may serve as a legal impediment.

In Hanafi legal theory, ignorance (jahl) is an acquired impediment that can affect the applicability of rulings. This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul classifies ignorance into four distinct types based on whether they excuse a person in the Hereafter or create a legal doubt (shubha) in worldly matters. Understanding these categories is essential for correctly applying Hanafi principles when evaluating the state of a person's knowledge and intention.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish self-caused vs. external impediments
The text first divides all acquired impediments into those arising from the person themselves (e.g., ignorance, intoxication) and those imposed by others (e.g., coercion). Locate this distinction in the passage.
2
Identify the four types of ignorance
Read the explicit enumeration: 1) false ignorance without doubt, no excuse in afterlife; 2) lesser false ignorance, also no excuse; 3) ignorance that serves as a valid doubt (shubha); 4) ignorance that serves as a valid excuse (udhr). Commit these to memory.
Type 1 is associated with kufr after clear proof.
3
Determine legal effect for each type
For each type, note its effect: Types 1 and 2 give no excuse in the afterlife; Type 3 creates a shubha that can block certain worldly punishments; Type 4 is a full excuse. Abu Hanifa's discussion of Type 1 shows that even though it is not an excuse in the afterlife, in worldly rulings it can act as a shubha that diverts the application of Shariah imperatives.
This step requires understanding the distinction between 'udhr (excuse) and shubha (doubt).
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning for Type 1
The text explains that the disbeliever's own conviction (dyana) serves as a 'repeller' of attribution and of Shariah proof in rulings that are subject to change. This is a divine strategy of gradual entrapment and preparation for punishment. The hadith 'al-dunya sijn al-mu'min wa jannat al-kafir' is cited as evidence.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle of treating disbelief as a shubha in mu'amalat (worldly transactions) despite its moral gravity.
5
Apply classification to a hypothetical
Consider a person who converts to Islam but is unaware of certain prohibitions (e.g., interest). Which type of ignorance applies? What is the legal effect? Type 4 likely applies: valid excuse. Contrast with a person who rejects clear Quranic injunctions out of arrogance – Type 1.
This step integrates the classification into practical istinbat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي نوعان: باب العوارض المكتسبة من المرء على نفسه، ومن غيره عليه. أما التي من جهته : فالجهل، والسُّكْرُ ، والهَزْلُ والسَّفَهُ، والخطأ، والسفر والذي من غيره عليه الإكراه. ***** فصل في الجهل، وهو القسم الأول. أما الجهل فأربعة أنواع: ١- جهل باطل بلا شبهة، لا يصلح عذراً أصلاً في الآخرة. ٢- وجهل هو دونه، لكنه باطل لا يصلح عذراً أيضاً في الآخرة. ٣- وجهل يصلح شبهةً . ٤- وجهل يصلح عذراً. أما الأول : فالكفر من الكافر لا يصلح عذراً؛ لأنه مكابرة وجحود بعد وضوح الدليل. واختلف في ديانة الكافر على خلاف حكم الإسلام. أما أبو حنيفة رحمه الله فقد قال: إنها تصلح دافعةً للتعرض، ودافعةً لدليل الشرع في الأحكام التي تحتمل التغير ، ليصير الخطاب قاصراً عنهم في أحكام الدنيا استدراجاً بهم ومكراً عليهم، وتركاً لهم على الجهل، وتمهيداً لعقاب الآخرة، والخلود في النار، وتحقيقاً لقول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام : «الدنيا سجن المؤمن وجنة الكافر».
And they are of two types: Chapter of Acquired Impediments from the person upon himself, and from others upon him. As for those from his own side: ignorance, intoxication, jest, foolishness, error, travel; and from others upon him: coercion. ***** Section on Ignorance, which is the first division. As for ignorance, it is of four types: 1- False ignorance without any doubt, not valid as an excuse at all in the afterlife. 2- Ignorance below that, but it is false, also not valid as an excuse in the afterlife. 3- Ignorance that serves as a valid doubt. 4- Ignorance that serves as an excuse. As for the first: the disbelief of the disbeliever is not a valid excuse because it is obstinacy and denial after clarity of proof. There is disagreement concerning the religion of the disbeliever contrary to the ruling of Islam. As for Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, he said: It serves as a repeller of attribution, and a repeller of the proof of Shariah in rulings that are subject to change, so that the discourse is deficient regarding them in worldly rulings, as a gradual entrapment of them and a plot against them, leaving them in ignorance, preparing for the punishment of the afterlife and eternal fire, and fulfilling the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'This world is a prison for the believer and a paradise for the disbeliever.'
Source pages p. 742

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul is a definitional section on acquired impediments, focusing on ignorance. The author first lists the two categories of impediments: self-caused (ignorance, intoxication, etc.) and external (coercion). Then he dedicates a section to ignorance, dividing it into four types based on their legal effect. Type 1: sheer obstinacy against clear proof—no excuse in this life or the Hereafter. Type 2: similar but less severe—still no excuse. Type 3: ignorance that creates a genuine doubt (shubha)—may affect rulings. Type 4: ignorance that is a valid excuse (e.g., a new Muslim). The author then elaborates on Type 1, stating that the disbelief of a disbeliever is not an excuse because it is stubborn denial. However, Abu Hanifa holds that the disbeliever's religious conviction itself serves as a doubt that repels the application of Shariah in certain worldly rulings that are subject to change. This is a divine strategy to give them respite and increase their punishment in the afterlife, as confirmed by the hadith, 'The world is a prison for the believer and a paradise for the disbeliever.' The hadith is used here as evidence that disbelievers enjoy worldly benefits, so leaving them in ignorance is part of divine justice.

Key Terms
الجهل (al-jahl): خفة تعرض الإنسان لعدم العلم حقيقة أو حكماً، وهو عارض مكتسب
الشبهة (al-shubha): ما يشبه الحق في الظن وهو باطل في نفس الأمر، يصلح لدفع العقوبة عند الحنفية
العذر (al-'udhr): ما يرفع المؤاخذة عن المكلف في الدنيا أو الآخرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
ما هو حكم النوع الأول من الجهل (جهل باطل بلا شبهة) في الآخرة عند الحنفية؟
أ) يصلح عذراً
ب) لا يصلح عذراً
ج) يصلح شبهة
د) يختلف حكمه بحسب الشخص

Key Takeaway
Ignorance in Hanafi usul is not monolithic: it ranges from inexcusable obstinacy to a valid excuse, with the intermediate category of shubha affecting worldly rulings.

When Ignorance Excuses in Hanafi Usul
Explaining when ignorance excuses in legal obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between cases where ignorance is a valid excuse and where it is not, by applying the Hanafi criteria of widespread propagation and hiddenness of the proof.

Core Idea
Once a ruling is widespread, ignorance is no longer an excuse — the burden shifts to the individual to seek knowledge.

Many assume that ignorance always excuses in Islamic law, but the Hanafi school conditions it on the nature of the proof. This lesson unpacks the principle: after a ruling becomes well-known in Dar al-Islam, notification is complete, and ignorance signals negligence. However, when the proof is hidden (khafi) and the act involves imposing a consequence on another, ignorance is accepted as a valid excuse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Locate the opening sentence: once the khitab becomes widespread, notification is complete. This tells us that ignorance afterward is due to the person's own shortcoming, not the proof's hiddenness.
The rule is absolute: widespread propagation ends the excuse of ignorance.
2
Recognize the exception for hidden proofs
The author lists cases where ignorance is excused: ignorance of the agent, pre-emptor, freed slave-girl, etc. Note the common feature: the dalil is khafi (hidden) and the act involves ilzam (imposition).
Contrast with the young virgin girl: her case is not excused because the proof is well-known.
3
Examine the evidentiary basis
The passage cites the hadith of Barirah to show that the option for a freed slave-girl is established by a hadith that is not widely known. This proves the hiddenness of the proof in that scenario.
The hadith is ahad in transmission, making it zanni al-thubut, but its meaning is clear.
4
Apply the criteria to new cases
Use the two-condition test: (1) Is the ruling widespread? If yes → no excuse. (2) If not widespread, is the proof hidden and is there imposition? If yes → excuse. Practice with the examples given.
The condition of 'adalah or number for the conveyor applies when there is ilzam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما إذا اشتهر الخطاب في دار الإسلام وانتشر : فقد تم التبليغ من صاحب الشرع ، فمَن جَهل من بعد : فإنما أُتي من قبل تقصيره، لا من قبل خفاء الدليل، فلا يُعذر.
و
كمَن لم يطلب الماء في العمران، ولكنه تيمم والماء موجود، فصلى: لم يُجزه.
و
وكذلك جهل الوكيل بالوكالة، وجهل المأذون بالإذن : يكون عذراً؛ لأن فيه ضَرْبَ إيجاب وإلزام، فلا بد من علمه، إلا أنه لا تُشترط فيمن يُبلّغه العدالة وإن كان فضولياً؛ لأنه ليس بإلزام محض، بل هو مخيّر .
وجهل الوكيل بالعزل وجهلُ المأذون بالحجر، وجهل مولى العبد الجاني فيما يتصرف فيه وجهل الشفيع بالشفعة يكون عذراً؛ لأن الدليل خفي، وفيه إلزام ، فشَرَطَ أبو حنيفة رحمه الله في الذي يُبلغه من غير رسالة العدالة، أو العدد.
و
وكذلك جهل المرأة البكر بإنكاح الولي مثله.
وكذلك قوله في تبليغ الشرائع إلى الحربي الذي أسلم في دار الحرب، ولم يهاجر إلينا، إذا لم يكن المبلغ رسول الإمام.
وكذلك جهلُ الأمة المنكوحة إذا أعتقت بالإعتاق، أو بالخيار بعد العلم بالإعتاق يُجعل عذراً؛ لأن الدليل خفي في حقها، ولأنها : دافعة.
بخلاف الصغيرة البكر إذا بلغت وقد أنكحها أخوها، فلم تعلم بالخيار لم تُعذر، وجُعل سكوتُها ،رضاً؛ لأن دليل العلم في حقها مشهور، غير مستور، ولأنها تريد بذلك إلزام الفسخ ابتداء، لا الدفع نفسها، والمعتقة تدفع الزيادة عن نفسها.
ولهذا افترق الخياران في شرط القضاء.
(۱) قوله : لأن الدليل خَفِيَ في حقها الدليل في ثبوت الخيار : ما رواه ابن سعد عن الشعبي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال لبريرة لما عَتَقَت: «قد عتق معك بضعك فاختاري». وما رواه الدارقطني عن عائشة رضي الله عنها أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال لبريرة : اذهبي فقد عتق معك بضعك». وما في الصحيحين عن عائشة رضي الله عنها أن بريرة عَتَقَت، فخيَّرها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من زوجها.
As for when the address becomes well-known in Dar al-Islam and spreads, then notification from the Lawgiver is complete. So whoever is ignorant afterwards, his deficiency comes from his own shortcoming, not from the hiddenness of the proof, so he is not excused.
And [it is] like someone who did not seek water in inhabited areas but performed tayammum while water was present and prayed: it does not suffice him.
And similarly, ignorance of the agent regarding agency, and ignorance of the authorized person regarding permission, is an excuse because it involves obligation and imposition, so his knowledge is necessary. However, integrity is not required in the one who conveys it, even if he is a meddler, because it is not pure imposition, but rather he is given choice.
Ignorance of the agent of dismissal, ignorance of the authorized person of interdiction, ignorance of the master of the slave who committed a crime regarding what he disposes of, and ignorance of the pre-emptor of pre-emption are excuses because the proof is hidden, and there is imposition. So Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) stipulated integrity or number in the one who conveys it without a message.
And similarly, ignorance of the virgin woman regarding her guardian giving her in marriage is like that.
And similarly, his statement regarding delivering the laws to a warner who accepted Islam in the dar al-harb and did not migrate to us when the conveyor is not the messenger of the Imam.
And similarly, ignorance of the married slave-girl if she is freed, regarding the emancipation or the option after knowledge of emancipation, is made an excuse because the proof is hidden in her case, and because she is repelling.
Contrary to the young virgin girl if she reaches puberty and her brother has married her off, and she does not know of the option, she is not excused, and her silence is considered consent because the proof of knowledge in her case is well-known, not hidden, and because she desires thereby to initiate annulment, not to repel [something from] herself. The freed woman repels the excess from herself.
For this reason, the two options differ regarding the condition of judgment.
(1) His statement: because the proof is hidden in her case, the proof for the establishment of the option is what Ibn Sa'd narrated from al-Sha'bi that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Barirah when she was freed: 'Your marriage has been freed with you, so choose.' And what al-Daraqutni narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said to Barirah: 'Go, your marriage has been freed with you.' And what is in the Sahihain from Aisha that Barirah was freed, and the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) gave her a choice regarding her husband.
Source pages p. 754

Commentary
This passage is a usul rule explaining the circumstances in which ignorance of a legal ruling constitutes an excuse and when it does not. The author first establishes a general principle: once a legal command (khitab) has become widespread and well-known throughout Dar al-Islam, notification from the Lawgiver is considered complete. Subsequent ignorance is therefore attributed to the individual's own negligence, not to concealment of the proof, and thus such ignorance is not accepted as an excuse. The example given is that of a person who performs tayammum (dry ablution) instead of using water in an inhabited area where water is available; such a person's prayer is invalid because his ignorance is due to his own failure to seek water. The passage then transitions to specific cases where ignorance is excused because the proof is hidden (khafi) and involves an element of imposition (ilzam). The author lists several examples: an agent ignorant of his agency (jahl al-wakil bi al-wikala), an authorized person ignorant of his authorization (jahl al-ma'dhun bi al-idhn), ignorance of dismissal, interdiction, the master of a slave who committed a crime, and the pre-emptor's ignorance of pre-emption. In all these, the proof is hidden, and the act involves imposition on another. For cases where the information is conveyed without a formal messenger, Abu Hanifa stipulated that the conveyor must be either of just character ('adala) or of a sufficient number to ensure reliability. The passage then contrasts the case of a married slave-girl who is freed and is given the option to stay with her husband or leave. Her ignorance of the option is excused because the proof (the hadith of Barirah) is hidden in her situation. On the other hand, a young virgin girl who reaches puberty and is married by her brother is not excused for ignorance of the option because the proof is well-known; her silence is deemed consent. The difference lies in the nature of the right: the freed slave-girl is repelling an excess (she wants to avoid additional marital ties), while the young girl seeks to initiate annulment. The passage ends with a footnote providing the hadith evidence for the slave-girl's option, citing reports about Barirah (may Allah be pleased with her).

Key Terms
الجهل (al-jahl): Ignorance of a legal ruling or fact that affects the validity of an action.
العذر (al-‘udhr): A valid excuse that prevents a person from being held blameworthy or that saves a legal act from nullity.
الدليل الخفي (al-dalil al-khafi): A legal proof that is not immediately apparent or commonly known, requiring investigation.
الإلزام (al-ilzam): Imposition of an obligation or legal consequence upon another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example where ignorance is NOT excused?
A) A pre-emptor ignorant of pre-emption
B) An agent ignorant of his dismissal
C) A person who performs tayammum in an area where water is available and widely known
D) A freed slave-girl ignorant of the option to leave her husband

Key Takeaway
Ignorance is a valid excuse only when the proof is hidden (khafi) and the act involves imposition (ilzam); once a ruling is widespread, ignorance is inexcusable.

Rescission Under Khiyar al-Shart Requires Knowledge of the Other Party
Stating a rule for rescission under option of stipulation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that rescission under khiyar al-shart requires the other party's knowledge and identify the exception for messenger communication.

Core Idea
Rescission under khiyar al-shart is an imposition on the other party; it is valid only with knowledge.

This passage from Al-Bazdawi's Usul explains a specific fiqh ruling derived from a broader principle about ignorance and imposition. It addresses a common scenario in sales: when a party has an option to rescind within three days, how must that rescission be executed? The Hanafi masters rule that it requires the other party's presence or knowledge, otherwise it is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is rescission (faskh) under khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation). The ruling is that it is invalid unless done in the presence (mahdar) of the other party.
The text states: 'if he rescinds the contract without the presence of his partner: that it is not valid except in his presence.'
2
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The option makes the contract non-binding (ghayr lazim) but does not automatically cause rescission. Rescission is an act that eliminates the binding nature and imposes (ilzam) on the other party. Therefore, it requires the other's knowledge.
The reasoning: 'the option is placed to exclude the ruling of the contract due to lack of choice... then it is rescinded for lack of bindingness, not that the option inevitably leads to rescission.'
3
Apply the condition of knowledge
The condition is that the rescission must be communicated to the other party, either by his presence or by a messenger.
Knowledge is the operative condition; presence is one means to achieve it.
4
Note the exception
If a messenger conveys the rescission within the three-day period, it is valid even if the messenger is not a just person ('adl).
The text: 'If the messenger of the holder of the option conveys it to him, it is valid within the three [days] without stipulation of integrity.'
5
Evaluate the practical consequence
If rescission is done secretly or without notification, the contract remains binding, and the rescinding party may be held to the sale.
This highlights the importance of clear communication in contractual options.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا الأصل قال أبو حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله في صاحب خيار الشرط في البيع إذا فسخ العقد بغير مَحضَرٍ من صاحبه: إن ذلك لا يصح إلا بمحضر منه ؛ لأن الخيار وضع لاستثناء حكم العقد لعدم الاختيار، فيصير العقد به غیر لازم ، ثم يُفسخ لفوات اللزوم ، لا أن الخيار للفسخ لا محالة، فيصير هذا بالفسخ متصرفاً على الآخر بما فيه إلزام، فلا يصح إلا بعلمه.
فإن بلغه رسولُ صاحب الخيار: صحَ في الثلاث بلا شرط عدالة.
And upon this principle, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them both) said regarding the holder of an option of stipulation in a sale, if he rescinds the contract without the presence of his partner: that it is not valid except in his presence. Because the option is placed to exclude the ruling of the contract due to lack of choice, so the contract becomes non-binding thereby, then it is rescinded for lack of bindingness, not that the option inevitably leads to rescission. Consequently, this person by rescinding becomes an administrator over the other with what involves imposition, so it is not valid except with his knowledge.
If the messenger of the holder of the option conveys it to him, it is valid within the three [days] without stipulation of integrity.
Source pages p. 755

Commentary
This passage presents a specific ruling derived from the earlier principle about ignorance and imposition. The issue concerns khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation) in a sale contract. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, if the party who has the right to rescind the contract (sahib al-khiyar) attempts to rescind without the presence (mahdar) of the other party, the rescission is invalid. The reasoning (ta'lil) given is that the option is established to exclude the binding effect of the contract due to the lack of full consent at the time of contracting. Thus, the contract becomes non-binding (ghayr lazim) through the option. However, rescission is an act that eliminates the contract's binding nature, not a necessary consequence of the option itself. Since rescission involves imposing a legal effect (ilzam) on the other party—namely, the annulment of his right—it is considered an act of disposition (tasarruf) that affects the other. Therefore, it is only valid if the other party has knowledge of it. The passage then adds an exception: if the option-holder sends a messenger to communicate the rescission within the three-day period, the rescission is valid even if the messenger is not a just person ('adl). This is because the messenger is merely conveying information, not imposing anything, so no strict conditions of 'adalah are required.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): A contractual option stipulating a period during which either party may rescind the sale.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract.
محضر (mahdar): Presence or being in the same location as the other party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require the presence of the other party for rescission under khiyar al-shart?
A) Because the option itself is a contract
B) Because rescission without presence is like a gift
C) Because rescission imposes on the other party and requires his knowledge
D) Because the messenger must be just

Key Takeaway
Rescission under khiyar al-shart requires the other party's knowledge; otherwise it is invalid, except that a messenger suffices without a condition of integrity.

The Legal Capacity of the Drunk Person: Utterances, Intent, and Hadd
Defining the legal status of a drunk person in Islamic law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that intoxication does not remove legal capacity (khitab) but negates intent (qasd), and distinguish between valid utterances and actions requiring true intent.

Core Idea
The drunk's utterances are valid; only his intent is negated.

This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi examines the legal status of a drunk person (sakran). It establishes that intoxication does not remove a person from being addressed by divine commands (khitab), as proven by Quran 4:43. However, it does negate intent (qasd). Therefore, all verbal statements (ibarah) are legally effective—divorce, sale, etc.—but actions requiring true belief, like apostasy, are not binding. The passage also discusses the application of hadd penalties and the role of istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule
Recognize that the drunk is still mukhatab (addressed by Shari'ah) as proven by the Quranic verse. This is the foundation for all subsequent rulings.
Note the dalil: Quran 4:43 addresses drunk people directly, implying they are accountable.
2
Distinguish utterance from intent
Understand that intoxication eliminates intent (qasd) but not the utterance (ibarah). This distinction is key: actions that depend solely on utterance (talaq, sale) are valid; actions that require true belief or intent (apostasy) are not.
This is a parsing exercise: separate the components of legal acts.
3
Apply the distinction to specific cases
Work through the examples given: divorce (valid), apostasy (invalid by istihsan), acknowledgment of debt (valid), hadd penalties (only after sobriety). Note which category each falls into.
The passage explicitly lists these cases; use them to verify your understanding.
4
Analyze the role of istihsan
Examine why istihsan is used to set aside strict qiyas in the apostasy case. Strict analogy would say all utterances are binding, but istihsan recognizes that apostasy requires a change of belief, which the drunk cannot genuinely manifest.
This demonstrates how istihsan operates as a secondary source in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يسكر منه، وذلك من جنس ما يُتلهى به فيصيرُ السُّكْرُ منه مثل السكر من الشراب المحرَّم، ألا يُرى أنه يوجب الحد.
وهذا السكر بالإجماع لا ينافي الخطاب قال الله تعالى: ﴿ يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا لَا تَقْرَبُواْ الصَّلَوةَ وَأَنتُمْ سُكَرَى ﴾ النساء/٤٣.
فإن كان هذا خطاباً في حال السكر: فلا شبهة فيه، وإن كان في حال الصحو فكذلك ألا يُرى أنه لا يقال للعاقل إذا جننت فلا تفعل كذا. وإذا ثبت أنه مخاطَب ثبت أن السكر لا يُبطل شيئاً من الأهلية، فتلزمه أحكامُ الشرع كلّها وتصح عباراته كلها بالطلاق والعتاق والبيع والشراء والأقارير.
وإنما ينعدم بالسكر القصد، دون العبارة، حتى إن السكران إذا تكلم بكلمة الكفر: لم تبن منه امرأتُه؛ استحساناً.
وإذا أسلم يجب أن يصح إسلامه، كإسلام المكره.
وإذا أقرَّ بالقصاص، أو باشر سبب القصاص: لزمه حكمه.
وإذا قَذَفَ، أو أقرَّ به لزمه الحد؛ لأن السكر دليل الرجوع، وذلك لا يبطل بصريحه فبدليله أولى.
وإذا زنى في سكره: حدَّ إذا صحا.
وإذا أقر أنه سكر من الخمر طائعاً لم يُحد حتى يصحو، فيُقرَّ، أو تقوم عليه البينة.
وإذا أقرَّ بشيءٍ من الحدود: لم يؤخذ به إلا بحد القذف.
وإنما لم يوضع عنه الخطاب، ولزمته أحكام الشرع؛ لأن السكر لا يزيل العقل، لكنه سرورٌ غَلَبَه.
فإن كان سببه معصية: لم يُعد عذراً.
وكذلك إذا كان مباحاً مقيَّداً، وهو مما يُتلهى به في الأصل، وإذا كان مباحاً مطلقاً: جعل عذراً.
وأما ما يعتمد الاعتقاد، فمثلُ الردة: فإن ذلك لا يثبت؛ استحساناً؛ لعدم ركنه؛ لأن السكر يُجعَل عذراً.
وأما ما يُبتنى على صحة العبارة: فقد وجد ركنه، والسكر لا يصلح عذراً.
وأما الحدود فإنما تُقام عليه إذا صحا؛ لما بينا أن السكر بعينه ليس بعذر ولا شبهة، إلا أن من عادة السكران اختلاط الكلام وهو أصله، ولا ثبات له على الكلام ألا يُرى أنهم اتفقوا أن السكر لا يثبت بدون هذا الحد.
وقد زاد عليه أبو حنيفة رحمه الله في حق الحد، فيحتمل أن يكون حده في غير الحد: هو أن يختلط كلامه، ويَهذي غالباً، وإذا كان ذلك: أُقيم السكر مقام حقيقة الرجوع، فلم يعمل فيما يعاين من أسباب الحد، وعَمِل في الإقرار الذي يحتمل الرجوع، ولم يعمل فيما لا يحتمله، وهو الإقرار بحد القذف والقصاص.
He becomes intoxicated from it, and that is of the kind that is used for amusement, so the intoxication from it becomes like intoxication from forbidden drink; do you not see that it necessitates the hadd penalty? And this intoxication by consensus does not contradict the divine address. Allah Most High said: 'O you who have believed, do not approach prayer while you are intoxicated' (4:43). If this is an address in the state of intoxication, there is no doubt about it. If it is in the state of sobriety, then likewise, do you not see that it is not said to a rational person: 'If you go mad, do not do such and such'? And when it is established that he is addressed, it is established that intoxication does not nullify anything of legal capacity. So all rulings of the Shari'ah are binding on him, and all his utterances are valid: divorce, manumission, sale, purchase, and acknowledgments. And only intention is eliminated by intoxication, not the utterance. So if a drunk person utters a word of disbelief, his wife does not separate from him, by way of istihsan. And if he embraces Islam, his Islam must be valid, like the Islam of one who is coerced. And if he acknowledges retaliation (qisas) or initiates a cause of qisas, its ruling is binding on him. And if he commits slander (qadhf) or acknowledges it, the hadd penalty is binding on him, because intoxication is an indication of taking back, and that is not nullified by explicit statement, so by its indication it is even more so. And if he commits zina while intoxicated, he is punished with hadd when he sobers up. And if he acknowledges that he intentionally drank wine, he is not punished until he sobers up and then acknowledges or evidence is established against him. And if he acknowledges something of the hadd penalties, it is not taken from him except in the case of the hadd of qadhf. And the address has not been removed from him, and the rulings of the Shari'ah are binding on him, because intoxication does not remove reason, but it is overwhelming joy. If its cause is disobedience, it is not considered an excuse. Likewise if it is a restricted permissible substance that is originally used for amusement. And if it is an unrestricted permissible substance, it is made an excuse. As for what depends on belief, like apostasy: that is not established, by istihsan, due to the lack of its pillar, because intoxication is made an excuse. As for what is based on the validity of utterance, its pillar is present, and intoxication does not qualify as an excuse. As for the hadd penalties, they are only imposed on him when he sobers up, because we have explained that intoxication itself is neither an excuse nor a doubt, except that the habit of the drunk person is confusion of speech—that is his original state—and he has no stability in speech. Do you not see that they have agreed that intoxication is not established without this limit? And Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) imposed an additional condition regarding hadd. It is possible that his definition for non-hadd matters is: that his speech is confused and he frequently hallucinates. When that is the case, intoxication is treated as the reality of retraction, so it does not apply to witnessed causes of hadd, but it applies to acknowledgment that admits of retraction, and it does not apply to what does not admit of retraction, namely acknowledgment of the hadd of qadhf and qisas.
Source pages p. 758

Commentary
This passage is a ruling outlining the legal status of a drunk person (sakran) in Hanafi law. It opens by affirming that intoxication from any intoxicating substance, whether forbidden or not, entails the hadd penalty and does not remove the person from being the addressee of divine commands (khitab). The Quranic verse (4:43) is cited as proof that even in drunkenness, Allah addresses the believers, so the drunk retains legal capacity (ahliyyah). Therefore, all Shari'ah rulings apply to him, and his verbal utterances—divorce, manumission, sales, acknowledgments—are valid. However, intention (qasd) is eliminated by intoxication; only the utterance remains. Hence, if a drunk utters disbelief, his wife does not separate from him (by istihsan) because apostasy requires sincere belief change, which is absent. But if he embraces Islam, it is valid, like the coerced person's Islam. For actions like retaliation (qisas) and slander (qadhf), his acknowledgment is binding because intoxication is only a presumptive indication of retraction, which does not override explicit statement. For hadd punishments like zina and drinking, the drunk is punished only after sobering up and confirming the acknowledgment or evidence. The passage then distinguishes between different types of intoxicants and their excuses: if the cause is disobedience (like drinking wine), it is no excuse; if it is a restricted permissible (like certain medicines used for amusement), also no excuse; but unrestricted permissible intoxicants (like some sedatives) are an excuse. Finally, it explains the Hanafi definition of drunkenness: Abu Hanifa holds that for hadd purposes, drunkenness is established when the person's speech is confused and he frequently hallucinates (ikhtilat al-kalam wa yahdhi ghaliban). This definition is used to determine whether intoxication can be treated as a retraction of an acknowledgment: it applies to acknowledgments that can be retracted (like those requiring hadd) but not to those that cannot be retracted (qadhf and qisas). The passage emphasizes that the drunk is fully responsible for his utterances but not for intentional acts due to absence of intent.

Key Terms
سكر (sukr): Intoxication: a state overwhelming a person with joy that does not remove reason but overpowers it.
خطاب (khitab): Divine address: the Quran and Sunnah commands directed at mukallaf (legally responsible persons).
قصد (qasd): Intent: the conscious, deliberate aim behind an action.
عبارة (ibarah): Utterance: the spoken word, regardless of intent behind it.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference: a method of departing from strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more equitable ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is invalid because the drunk lacks intention.
B) It is valid because the utterance is effective regardless of intent.
C) It is suspended until the person sobers up.
D) It is valid only if the intoxicant was from a permissible substance.

Key Takeaway
The drunk retains full legal capacity for verbal acts; only his intent is negated.
