Hazl (Jest) in Hanafi Usul: Definition, Effect, and Ruling Categories
Defining hazl and its legal consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define hazl in Hanafi usul, state its legal effect, explain its condition, and apply the two-fold division for deriving rulings.

Core Idea
Hazl removes consent for the ruling but not for the direct act—like khiyar al-shart—and does not affect capacity.

This passage from Bazdawi's Usul introduces hazl (jest) as a distinct category of speech. While jest may seem trivial, Hanafi usul gives it a precise legal effect: it negates consent for the resulting ruling but not for the act of uttering the statement. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly analyzing contracts and acknowledgments made in jest.

Guided Path
1
Define hazl and jadd
Read the first two sentences: hazl is intending something for what it was not placed for; jadd is the opposite. Memorize these definitions as foundational.
Hazl = play; jadd = seriousness.
2
Identify the legal effect of hazl
The passage states hazl removes choice and consent regarding the ruling (hukm) but not regarding the direct act (mubasharah). Compare to khiyar al-shart in sale: both negate consent for the contract's effect but not for the act of contracting.
Effect is on consent for the ruling, not on the act itself.
3
Explain the condition of hazl
Hazl must be explicit in speech (sarih bi al-lisan), but unlike khiyar al-shart, it does not need to be mentioned in the contract itself. Note that talji'ah (simulated coercion) is treated identically.
Condition is oral explicitness, not in-contract mention.
4
Understand the two-fold division for deriving rulings
The passage says rulings must be derived based on whether hazl enters into something that admits annulment (e.g., revocable contracts) or not (e.g., binding obligations), and similarly for acknowledgments (iqrar) of rescindable vs. non-rescindable matters. This framework guides the jurist in applying the hazl doctrine.
Two aspects: matter admits annulment or not; acknowledgment can be rescinded or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الهزل، وهو القسم الثالث الهزل : فتفسيره اللعب وهو أن يُراد بالشيء ما لم يوضع له. وهو ضد الجد، وهو أن يراد بالشيء ما وُضع له. فصار الهزل ينافي اختيار الحكم والرضا به ولا ينافي الرضا بالمباشرة، واختيار المباشرة، فصار بمعنى خيار الشرط في البيع أنه يُعدم الرضا والاختيار جميعاً في حق الحكم، ولا يُعدم الرضا والاختيار في حق المباشرة[؟] هذا تفسير الهزل، وأثره. وشرطه أن يكون صريحاً مشروطاً باللسان، إلا أنه لا يُشترط ذكره : في نفس العقد ، بخلاف خيار الشرط. والتلجئة : هي الهزل، وإذا كان كذلك : لم يكن منافياً للأهلية، ولا لوجوب شيء من الأحكام، ولا عذراً في وضع الخطاب بحال، لكنه لما كان أثره ما قلنا وجب النظر في الأحكام كيف تنقسم في حكم الرضا والاختيار ؟ فيجب تخريجها على هذا الحد، وذلك على وجوه: ١- إما أن يدخل الهزلُ والتلجئةُ فيما لا يحتمل النقض، أو فيما يحتمله، فهذا وجه. ۲- ووجه آخر : أن يدخل على الإقرار بما ينفسخ ، أو لا.
Section on hazl (jest), which is the third division. Hazl: its interpretation is play, and it is that something is intended for what it was not placed for. It is the opposite of jadd (seriousness), which is that something is intended for what it was placed for. Thus hazl contradicts the choice of the ruling and consent to it, and does not contradict consent to the direct act and choice of the direct act. So it becomes similar to khiyar al-shart (option of condition) in sale in that it eliminates consent and choice entirely regarding the ruling, and does not eliminate consent and choice regarding the direct act. This is the interpretation of hazl and its effect. Its condition is that it be explicit, conditioned on the tongue, except that its mention is not required in the contract itself, unlike khiyar al-shart. And talji'ah (coercion simulation) is hazl. When it is like that, it is not contrary to legal capacity, nor to the obligation of any rulings, nor an excuse in the address of the Lawgiver at all. But since its effect is as we said, it is necessary to consider how rulings divide regarding consent and choice. So they must be derived according to this definition, and that in aspects: 1) Either hazl and talji'ah enter into what does not admit annulment, or into what admits it; this is one aspect. 2) Another aspect: they enter into an acknowledgment (iqrar) of what can be rescinded, or not.
Source pages p. 760

Commentary
This passage defines hazl (jest) as the third category (likely after seriousness and error). Hazl is when one intends a meaning other than the literal meaning of the words; it is the opposite of jadd (seriousness). The key legal effect is that hazl removes the element of choice and consent regarding the legal ruling (the effect of the contract) but does not remove consent regarding the physical act of making the statement. This is analogous to khiyar al-shart in sales, where the option condition negates consent for the contract's binding effect but not for the act of contracting. The condition for hazl is that it must be explicit in speech, but unlike khiyar al-shart, it does not need to be mentioned within the contract. Talji'ah (a form of duress simulation) is treated the same as hazl. Importantly, hazl does not affect legal capacity (ahliyyah) nor does it exempt one from obligations, nor is it an excuse from the divine address. However, its effect on consent requires that rulings be analyzed based on two considerations: (1) whether the hazl relates to something that can be annulled (like a revocable contract) or not (like a binding contract), and (2) whether it applies to an acknowledgment of something that can be rescinded or not (like debt vs. hudud). This aligns with the Hanafi usul principle that intent matters for the ruling but not for the action's validity.

Key Terms
هزل (hazl): Play or jest; intending a meaning other than what the words are placed for.
تلجئة (talji'ah): A form of hazl (simulated coercion) where one creates a situation of apparent duress; treated identically to hazl.
جد (jadd): Seriousness; intending the literal meaning of the words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the condition for hazl to have legal effect?
A) It must be indicated by circumstances, not just words.
B) It must be explicit in speech, and its mention is required in the contract.
C) It must be explicit in speech, but its mention is not required in the contract.
D) It must be written, not spoken.

Key Takeaway
Hazl is a valid consideration in Hanafi usul: it negates consent for the ruling but not for the act, and its effect depends on whether the underlying matter admits annulment or rescission.

Scope of Jest in Contracts: Belief vs. Rescission
Classifying contracts and modes of jest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between contracts founded on belief and those subject to rescission, and identify the three dimensions of jest (essence, amount, type) that affect the contract.

Core Idea
Jest does not affect contracts of belief but can affect contracts of exchange, depending on its object.

This lesson explores the Hanafi classification of contracts regarding the effect of jest. Al-Bazdawi distinguishes between contracts built on belief (like marriage) and those that are susceptible to rescission (like sale). Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining when a jesting party's words carry legal weight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of contracts
The passage divides contracts into those based on belief (faith and apostasy) and those subject to rescission (sale, lease). Recognize that marriage falls into the former and is immune to jest.
This is derived from the hadith about marriage, divorce, and return.
2
Classify the dimensions of jest
For contracts subject to rescission, jest can affect the essence, amount, or type of consideration. Note each dimension.
3
Apply the legal definition of hazl
Using the legal_definition_dissector, understand that hazl removes the party's intention for the legal effect, not the outward act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه آخر أن يدخل فيما يُبتنى على الاعتقاد، وذلك وجهان: الإيمان، والردة. فأما إذا دخل فيما يحتمل النقض مثل البيع والإجارة، وذلك على ثلاثة أوجه: إما أن يهزلا بأصله، أو بقدر العوض، أو بجنسه. وكلُّ وجه على أربعة أوجه: إما أن يتواضعا على الهزل، ثم يتفقا على الإعراض، أو على البناء، أو على أن لا يحضرهما شيء، أو يختلفا. فأما إذا تواضعا على الهزل بأصله، ثم اتفقا على البناء: فإن البيع منعقد؛ لما قلنا إن الهازل مختار راض بمباشرة السبب، لكنه غير مختار ولا راض بحكمه، وكان بمنزلة خيار الشرط مؤبداً، فانعقد العقد فاسداً، غير موجب للملك، كخيار المتبايعين معاً، على احتمال الجواز. كرجل باع عبداً على أنه بالخيار أبداً، أو على أنهما بالخيار أبداً، فإن نقضه أحدهما: انتقض، وإن أجازاه: جاز. وعند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله : يجب أن يكون مقدراً بالثلاث، ولهذا يقع الملك بهذا البيع وإن اتصل به القبض. (۱) ودلالة هذه الجملة : أن الهزل لا يؤثر في النكاح بالسنة (١) فعلم به أنه لا ينافي الإيجاب، وإنما دخل على الحكم. وأما إذا اتفقا على الإعراض : فإن البيع صحيح، وقد بطل الهزل بإعراضهما عن المواضعة؛ لأن حقيقة العقد تحتمل الفسخ، فالمواضعة التي دونه : أولى. وإن بَنَيًا على المواضعة فسد العقد بالاتفاق؛ لأنهما قصدا الفساد به، وحققا البناء عليه. وإن اتفقا على أنهما لم يحضرهما شيء، أو اختلفا في البناء والإعراض: فإن العقد صحيح عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله في الحالين، فجعل أبو حنيفة رحمه الله صحة الإيجاب أولى إذا سكتا، وكذلك إذا اختلفا. وقال : صحيح.
Another aspect: that [the effect of jest] enters into what is built upon belief, and that is two aspects: faith and apostasy. As for entering into what is susceptible to rescission like sale and lease, that is in three ways: either they jest in its essence, or in the amount of the compensation, or in its type. And each way has four aspects: either they mutually agree on jest, then agree on abandonment, or on building upon it, or that nothing occurs to them, or they differ. As for when they mutually agree on jest in its essence, then agree on building upon it: then the sale is concluded; because we said that the jester is voluntarily choosing to perform the cause, but he is not voluntarily choosing nor pleased with its effect, and it is like an eternal option condition, so the contract is concluded as void, not effecting ownership, like the option of both parties together, on the possibility of permissibility. Like a man selling a slave on condition that he has an eternal option, or that both have an eternal option: if one of them rescinds, it is rescinded; if both validate, it is valid. According to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him: it must be limited to three [days], and for this reason ownership occurs through this sale even if possession is connected to it. (1) And the indication of this sentence: that jest does not affect marriage by the Sunnah, so it is known that it does not contradict the offer, but only enters upon the effect. And as for when they agree on abandonment: then the sale is valid, and the jest is nullified by their abandonment of the agreement; because the reality of the contract admits rescission, so the agreement beneath it is more fitting. And if they build upon the agreement, then the contract is void by consensus; because they intended corruption by it and actualized building upon it. And if they agree that nothing occurred to them, or they differ regarding building and abandonment: then the contract is valid according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, in both cases. Abu Hanifa considered the validity of the offer more probable when they both remain silent, and likewise when they differ. And he said: valid.
Source pages p. 761

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul examines the impact of jest (hazl) on contracts. The author distinguishes between contracts founded on belief (such as marriage and apostasy) and those open to rescission (like sale and lease). For sale contracts, jest can affect three elements: the essence of the contract, the amount of consideration, or its type. For each, four scenarios arise after the initial jest: (1) both parties later agree to build upon the contract, (2) both abandon it, (3) both remain silent, or (4) they differ. When they build upon the jest, the contract is concluded but void (fasid) because the jester intended the physical act but not its legal effect, akin to a contract with an eternal option. However, Abu Hanifa restricts such an option to three days; beyond that, ownership occurs if possession is taken. If both abandon the jest, the sale becomes valid, as the contract's nature allows reversal and the jest is overridden. If they build on the jest, it is void by consensus. Finally, if they are silent or disagree, Abu Hanifa deems the contract valid, prioritizing the offer's apparent sincerity. The hadith 'Three things are serious whether done in jest or in earnest: marriage, divorce, and return' is cited to show that jest does not affect marriage, establishing that jest does not negate the offer but only the ruling. This analysis highlights the Hanafi method of deriving rulings from ahadith and applying nuanced intention-based logic.

Key Terms
الهزل (al-hazl): Jest or playful speech that contradicts the serious intention of a legal act.
المواضعة (al-muwada'a): The mutual agreement or understanding between parties to engage in jest.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated in a contract allowing one or both parties to rescind within a specified period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Essence (أصل)
B) Type (جنس)
C) Quantity of the item
D) Amount of compensation (قدر العوض)

Key Takeaway
Jest only affects contracts that are subject to rescission, and even then, its effect depends on what aspect of the contract was jested.

Rulings on Post-Jest Intentions and the Eternal Option Analogy
Rulings for post-jest scenarios and eternal option.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the four post-jest scenarios (build, abandon, silence, differ) to determine the validity of a sale contract, and explain Abu Hanifa's limitation of the option period to three days.

Core Idea
Building upon jest makes a sale void by consensus; abandoning it makes it valid; silence or disagreement leaves the contract valid according to Abu Hanifa.

This lesson delves into the detailed rulings after a jest has occurred. The passage uses the analogy of an eternal option to explain that when both parties intend to build upon the jest, the contract is void because they intend corruption. Abu Hanifa's unique position limits the option period to three days, which affects ownership.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four post-jest scenarios
After a mutual agreement to jest, the parties may: abandon, build upon, remain silent, or differ. Identify each.
2
Apply the ruling for building upon the jest
If both parties agree to build upon the jest, the contract is concluded but void (fasid) because they intended corruption. This is compared to a sale with an eternal option.
3
Analyze the eternal option analogy
The passage likens jest-built contracts to those with an eternal option. Abu Hanifa requires such options to be limited to three days; otherwise ownership occurs after possession. Understand the ikhtilaf.
The opposing view allows eternal options.
4
Derive from the hadith
The hadith 'Three things are serious...' indicates that jest does not affect marriage, divorce, and return. This shows that jest only enters the effect (hukm) not the offer (ijab).
5
Determine the ruling for the remaining scenarios
If they abandon, the sale is valid; if silent or differ, Abu Hanifa rules valid. He prioritizes the apparent sincerity of the offer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه آخر أن يدخل فيما يُبتنى على الاعتقاد، وذلك وجهان: الإيمان، والردة. فأما إذا دخل فيما يحتمل النقض مثل البيع والإجارة، وذلك على ثلاثة أوجه: إما أن يهزلا بأصله، أو بقدر العوض، أو بجنسه. وكلُّ وجه على أربعة أوجه: إما أن يتواضعا على الهزل، ثم يتفقا على الإعراض، أو على البناء، أو على أن لا يحضرهما شيء، أو يختلفا. فأما إذا تواضعا على الهزل بأصله، ثم اتفقا على البناء: فإن البيع منعقد؛ لما قلنا إن الهازل مختار راض بمباشرة السبب، لكنه غير مختار ولا راض بحكمه، وكان بمنزلة خيار الشرط مؤبداً، فانعقد العقد فاسداً، غير موجب للملك، كخيار المتبايعين معاً، على احتمال الجواز. كرجل باع عبداً على أنه بالخيار أبداً، أو على أنهما بالخيار أبداً، فإن نقضه أحدهما: انتقض، وإن أجازاه: جاز. وعند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله : يجب أن يكون مقدراً بالثلاث، ولهذا يقع الملك بهذا البيع وإن اتصل به القبض. (۱) ودلالة هذه الجملة : أن الهزل لا يؤثر في النكاح بالسنة (١) فعلم به أنه لا ينافي الإيجاب، وإنما دخل على الحكم. وأما إذا اتفقا على الإعراض : فإن البيع صحيح، وقد بطل الهزل بإعراضهما عن المواضعة؛ لأن حقيقة العقد تحتمل الفسخ، فالمواضعة التي دونه : أولى. وإن بَنَيًا على المواضعة فسد العقد بالاتفاق؛ لأنهما قصدا الفساد به، وحققا البناء عليه. وإن اتفقا على أنهما لم يحضرهما شيء، أو اختلفا في البناء والإعراض: فإن العقد صحيح عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله في الحالين، فجعل أبو حنيفة رحمه الله صحة الإيجاب أولى إذا سكتا، وكذلك إذا اختلفا. وقال : صحيح.
Another aspect: that [the effect of jest] enters into what is built upon belief, and that is two aspects: faith and apostasy. As for entering into what is susceptible to rescission like sale and lease, that is in three ways: either they jest in its essence, or in the amount of the compensation, or in its type. And each way has four aspects: either they mutually agree on jest, then agree on abandonment, or on building upon it, or that nothing occurs to them, or they differ. As for when they mutually agree on jest in its essence, then agree on building upon it: then the sale is concluded; because we said that the jester is voluntarily choosing to perform the cause, but he is not voluntarily choosing nor pleased with its effect, and it is like an eternal option condition, so the contract is concluded as void, not effecting ownership, like the option of both parties together, on the possibility of permissibility. Like a man selling a slave on condition that he has an eternal option, or that both have an eternal option: if one of them rescinds, it is rescinded; if both validate, it is valid. According to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him: it must be limited to three [days], and for this reason ownership occurs through this sale even if possession is connected to it. (1) And the indication of this sentence: that jest does not affect marriage by the Sunnah, so it is known that it does not contradict the offer, but only enters upon the effect. And as for when they agree on abandonment: then the sale is valid, and the jest is nullified by their abandonment of the agreement; because the reality of the contract admits rescission, so the agreement beneath it is more fitting. And if they build upon the agreement, then the contract is void by consensus; because they intended corruption by it and actualized building upon it. And if they agree that nothing occurred to them, or they differ regarding building and abandonment: then the contract is valid according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, in both cases. Abu Hanifa considered the validity of the offer more probable when they both remain silent, and likewise when they differ. And he said: valid.
Source pages p. 761

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul examines the impact of jest (hazl) on contracts. The author distinguishes between contracts founded on belief (such as marriage and apostasy) and those open to rescission (like sale and lease). For sale contracts, jest can affect three elements: the essence of the contract, the amount of consideration, or its type. For each, four scenarios arise after the initial jest: (1) both parties later agree to build upon the contract, (2) both abandon it, (3) both remain silent, or (4) they differ. When they build upon the jest, the contract is concluded but void (fasid) because the jester intended the physical act but not its legal effect, akin to a contract with an eternal option. However, Abu Hanifa restricts such an option to three days; beyond that, ownership occurs if possession is taken. If both abandon the jest, the sale becomes valid, as the contract's nature allows reversal and the jest is overridden. If they build on the jest, it is void by consensus. Finally, if they are silent or disagree, Abu Hanifa deems the contract valid, prioritizing the offer's apparent sincerity. The hadith 'Three things are serious whether done in jest or in earnest: marriage, divorce, and return' is cited to show that jest does not affect marriage, establishing that jest does not negate the offer but only the ruling. This analysis highlights the Hanafi method of deriving rulings from ahadith and applying nuanced intention-based logic.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated in a contract allowing one or both parties to rescind within a specified period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Bazdawi, what is the ruling when the parties jest and then differ about whether to build or abandon?
A) Void
B) Valid
C) Suspended
D) Voidable

Key Takeaway
The post-jest intention critically determines the contract's validity: building upon jest voids it, while abandonment, silence, or disagreement leaves it valid.

Categorizing Transactions by Financial Element: Ruling on Jest for Non-Financial Acts
Defining three transaction types and ruling on jest for non-financial acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi categorization of transactions based on financial element and derive the ruling for jest in non-financial acts using the hadith of 'three things'.

Core Idea
Jest in acts with no financial element (divorce, manumission, etc.) is binding because the cause chosen is effective immediately.

This lesson begins by introducing a fundamental tripartite classification of transactions in Hanafi usul: those with no financial element, those where financial element is subsidiary, and those where it is primary. We will focus on the first category, examining the textual and rational proofs that make jest legally irrelevant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Read the opening lines that list transactions with no financial element (divorce, manumission, pardon for qisas, oath, vow).
This classification recurs in later fiqh discussions.
2
Analyze the hadith as dalil
The hadith 'Three things their seriousness is serious and their jest is serious: marriage, divorce, and oath' provides the primary proof. Note that marriage is included here but later treated separately due to its financial aspect.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted by Hanafis.
3
Understand the rational argument
The jester chooses the cause (sabab) but rejects the effect (hukm); however, these causes produce rulings that cannot be deferred or annulled (e.g., no option condition). Hence, jest is disregarded.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) based on the nature of the cause.
4
Derive the ruling
Combine the hadith and rational argument to conclude that in non-financial acts, jest does not affect validity.
The ruling is that the act is valid (sahih) and binds the jester.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما لا مال فيه أصلاً. وما كان المال فيه تبعاً. وما كان المال فيه مقصوداً. أما الذي لا مالَ فيه أصلاً وهو الطلاق والعتاق والعفو عن القصاص واليمين والنذر، وذلك كله صحيح، والهزل باطل بقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «ثلاث جدهنَّ جِد، وهَزَلُهنَّ جد: النكاح والطلاق واليمين». ولأن الهازل مختار للسبب، راض به دون حكمه، وحكم هذه الأسباب لا يحتمل الردَّ والتراخي، ألا يُرى أنه لا يحتمل خيار الشرط. وأما الذي يكون المال فيه تبعاً، مثل النكاح، فعلى أوجه: إما أن يهزلا بأصله، أو بقدر البدل، أو بجنسه. أما الهزل بأصله: فباطل، والعقد لازم. وأما الهزل بالقدر فيه: فإن اتفقا على الإعراض: فإن المهر ألفان، وإن اتفقا على البناء: فالمهر ألف. بخلاف مسألة البيع عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله؛ لأنه بالشرط الفاسد يفسد، والنكاح بمثله لا يفسد. وإن اتفقا على أنه لم يحضرهما شيء، أو اختلفا: فإن محمداً رحمه الله ذكر عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله أن النكاح جائز بألف، بخلاف البيع؛ لأن المهر تابع في هذا، فلا يُجعل مقصوداً بالصحة. وروى أبو يوسف عن أبي حنيفة رحمهما الله: أن المهر ألفان، فإن التسمية في الصحة مثل ابتداء البيع وفي ابتداء البيع جعل أبو حنيفة رحمه الله العمل بصحة الإيجاب أولى من العمل بصحة المواضعة، فكذلك هذا، وهذا أصح. وأما إذا تواضعا على الدنانير، وعلى أن المهر في الحقيقة دراهم، فإن اتفقا على الإعراض فالمهر على ما سميا، وإن اتفقا على البناء: وجب مهر المثل، بالإجماع، بخلاف البيع؛ لأنه لا يصح إلا بتسمية الثمن، والنكاح يصح بلا تسمية. وإن اتفقا على أنه لم يحضرهما شيء، أو اختلفا: فعلى رواية محمد رحمه الله: وجب مهر المثل بلا خلاف. وعلى رواية أبي يوسف عن أبي حنيفة رحمهما الله: يجب المسمى، وبطلت المواضعة. وعندهما يجب مهر المثل. وأما الذي يكون المال فيه مقصوداً، مثل الخلع، والعتق على مال، والصلح عن دم العمد: فإن ذلك على هذه الأوجه أيضاً: فإن هَزَلا بأصله، واتفقا على البناء فقد ذَكَرَ محمد رحمه الله في كتاب الإكراه في الخلع أن الطلاق واقع والمال لازم، وهذا عندنا قول أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله. فأما عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله: فإن الطلاق لا يقع؛ لأنه بمنزلة خيار
What has no financial element at all. And what has a financial element as subsidiary. And what has a financial element as primary. As for what has no financial element at all, which is divorce, manumission, pardon for qisas, oath, and vow: all of that is valid, and jest is invalid by his statement (saw): 'Three things their seriousness is serious and their jest is serious: marriage, divorce, and the oath.' And because the jester chooses the cause, is pleased with it without its effect, and the ruling of these causes does not admit of rejection or delay, do you not see that it does not admit of the option of condition? As for what has a financial element as subsidiary, such as marriage, it is in aspects: either they jest in its essence, or in the amount of the substitute, or in its type. As for jest in its essence: it is invalid, and the contract is binding. As for jest in the amount: if they agree on disregard, then the dower is two thousand; if they agree on stipulation, the dower is one thousand. Unlike the case of sale according to Abu Hanifa; because with a corrupt condition it becomes corrupt, but marriage does not become corrupt by the like. If they agree that nothing occurred to them, or they differ: then Muhammad narrated from Abu Hanifa that the marriage is valid with one thousand, unlike sale; because the dower is subsidiary in this, so it is not made primary for soundness. Abu Yusuf narrated from Abu Hanifa that the dower is two thousand; for the naming in soundness is like the beginning of sale, and in the beginning of sale Abu Hanifa made acting upon the soundness of the offer more appropriate than acting upon the soundness of the collusion, so likewise this, and this is more correct. As for if they collude on dinars, while the dower in reality is dirhams: if they agree on disregard, then the dower is as they named; if they agree on stipulation, the dower of the like is obligatory by consensus, unlike sale; because it is not valid except by naming the price, and marriage is valid without naming. If they agree that nothing occurred to them, or they differ: then according to the narration of Muhammad: the dower of the like is obligatory without dispute. According to the narration of Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa: the named is obligatory, and the collusion is void. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the dower of the like is obligatory. As for what has a financial element as primary, such as khul', manumission for money, and settlement for intentional homicide: it also falls under these aspects. If they jest in its essence and agree on stipulation, Muhammad mentioned in the book of coercion regarding khul' that divorce occurs and the money is binding, and this according to us is the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. As for according to Abu Hanifa: divorce does not occur; because it is like the option...
Source pages p. 765

Commentary
This passage, taken from a work on usul al-fiqh, presents a detailed fiqh ruling on the effect of jest (hazl) on different types of transactions. The author first categorizes transactions based on the presence and role of financial consideration: those with no financial element at all (e.g., divorce, manumission, pardon for qisas, oath, vow), those where financial element is subsidiary (e.g., marriage), and those where financial element is primary (e.g., khul', manumission for money, settlement for homicide). For the first category, the ruling is that such acts are valid and jest does not affect them, based on the hadith that 'three things are serious in both jest and seriousness: marriage, divorce, and the oath.' The rationale is that the jester chooses the cause (e.g., pronouncing divorce) but only intends the form without the legal effect; however, these causes produce effects that cannot be postponed or annulled (they admit no option clause). For the second category (marriage), the author examines subcases: jest in the essence of marriage (e.g., saying 'I marry you' jokingly) renders the jest invalid but the contract binding; jest in the amount of dower leads to different rulings depending on the parties' agreement: if they both disregard the jest (i'tirad), the dower is the higher stated amount (e.g., two thousand), but if they insist on the jest (bina'), the dower is the lower amount (one thousand). This contrasts with sale where a corrupt condition corrupts the contract. For marriage, the dower is subsidiary, so the contract remains valid even if the dower is disputed. The passage further presents disagreements between the Hanafi imams: Abu Hanifa's own position (as narrated by Muhammad or Abu Yusuf) and the position of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). In some cases, when collusion (tawada') occurs (e.g., pretending dower is dinars but actually dirhams), rules of dower of the like apply. For the third category (primary financial element), the ruling follows similar aspects, with ikhtilaf: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, divorce is valid and money is due; according to Abu Hanifa, divorce does not occur (text cuts off before completion). The passage demonstrates how Hanafi law uses hadith and rational argument to derive nuanced rulings, and how internal disagreements are recorded.

Key Terms
الهزل (al-hazl): Jest or joking in a legal transaction, where the speaker utters the words of a contract without intending its legal effect.
الطلاق (al-talaq): Divorce, the dissolution of marriage by the husband's pronouncement.
السبب (al-sabab): The legal cause or means that triggers a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling when a person jokingly makes an oath (yamin)?
A) The oath is invalid because it was made in jest.
B) The oath is valid and binding.
C) The oath is suspended until the person confirms intention.
D) The oath is valid only if taken seriously by others.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, jest in acts with no financial element (divorce, manumission, etc.) produces full legal effect because the cause itself is binding and immediate.

Jest in Marriage: Subsidiary Financial Element and Effect on Dower
Analyzing subcases of jest in marriage and internal Hanafi rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for jest in marriage subcases and distinguish between jest in essence and jest in amount.

Core Idea
Jest in the essence of marriage is ignored but contract binds; jest in dower amount leads to dower of 2000 (i'rad) or 1000 (bina') depending on agreement.

We now examine the second category: marriage, where financial element (dower) is subsidiary. The passage details subcases: jest in the essence of marriage, jest in the amount of dower, and jest in the type of dower. The ruling on dower is nuanced and depends on the parties' subsequent agreement, contrasting with sale where a corrupt condition corrupts the contract.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish jest in essence vs. jest in amount
Jest in essence (e.g., 'I marry you' jokingly) makes the jest invalid but the marriage binding. Jest in amount (e.g., pretending a dower of 1000 when they intend 2000) leads to different outcomes.
The difference is between jokingly entering the contract vs. jokingly setting a term.
2
Understand i'rad (disregard) and bina' (stipulation)
If both parties later agree to disregard the jest (i'rad), the higher named dower (2000) applies. If they agree to adhere to the jest (bina'), the lower amount (1000) applies.
This is based on prioritizing the apparent contract or the hidden agreement.
3
Contrast with sale
In sale, a corrupt condition (like a jest about price) corrupts the contract. But marriage is not corrupted by a corrupt condition; the contract remains valid and the dower issue is resolved separately.
This shows the principle that marriage is less susceptible to invalidity due to subsidiary issues.
4
Note the two narrations from Abu Hanifa
When parties differ or have no recollection, Muhammad narrates that dower is 1000 (lower), while Abu Yusuf narrates that it is 2000 (higher). The text declares Abu Yusuf's narration 'more correct' (asahh).
This tarjih indicates the stronger chain in the Hanafi school.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What has no financial element at all. And what has a financial element as subsidiary. And what has a financial element as primary. As for what has no financial element at all, which is divorce, manumission, pardon for qisas, oath, and vow: all of that is valid, and jest is invalid by his statement (saw): 'Three things their seriousness is serious and their jest is serious: marriage, divorce, and the oath.' And because the jester chooses the cause, is pleased with it without its effect, and the ruling of these causes does not admit of rejection or delay, do you not see that it does not admit of the option of condition? As for what has a financial element as subsidiary, such as marriage, it is in aspects: either they jest in its essence, or in the amount of the substitute, or in its type. As for jest in its essence: it is invalid, and the contract is binding. As for jest in the amount: if they agree on disregard, then the dower is two thousand; if they agree on stipulation, the dower is one thousand. Unlike the case of sale according to Abu Hanifa; because with a corrupt condition it becomes corrupt, but marriage does not become corrupt by the like. If they agree that nothing occurred to them, or they differ: then Muhammad narrated from Abu Hanifa that the marriage is valid with one thousand, unlike sale; because the dower is subsidiary in this, so it is not made primary for soundness. Abu Yusuf narrated from Abu Hanifa that the dower is two thousand; for the naming in soundness is like the beginning of sale, and in the beginning of sale Abu Hanifa made acting upon the soundness of the offer more appropriate than acting upon the soundness of the collusion, so likewise this, and this is more correct. As for if they collude on dinars, while the dower in reality is dirhams: if they agree on disregard, then the dower is as they named; if they agree on stipulation, the dower of the like is obligatory by consensus, unlike sale; because it is not valid except by naming the price, and marriage is valid without naming. If they agree that nothing occurred to them, or they differ: then according to the narration of Muhammad: the dower of the like is obligatory without dispute. According to the narration of Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa: the named is obligatory, and the collusion is void. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the dower of the like is obligatory. As for what has a financial element as primary, such as khul', manumission for money, and settlement for intentional homicide: it also falls under these aspects. If they jest in its essence and agree on stipulation, Muhammad mentioned in the book of coercion regarding khul' that divorce occurs and the money is binding, and this according to us is the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. As for according to Abu Hanifa: divorce does not occur; because it is like the option...
Source pages p. 765

Commentary
This passage, taken from a work on usul al-fiqh, presents a detailed fiqh ruling on the effect of jest (hazl) on different types of transactions. The author first categorizes transactions based on the presence and role of financial consideration: those with no financial element at all (e.g., divorce, manumission, pardon for qisas, oath, vow), those where financial element is subsidiary (e.g., marriage), and those where financial element is primary (e.g., khul', manumission for money, settlement for homicide). For the first category, the ruling is that such acts are valid and jest does not affect them, based on the hadith that 'three things are serious in both jest and seriousness: marriage, divorce, and the oath.' The rationale is that the jester chooses the cause (e.g., pronouncing divorce) but only intends the form without the legal effect; however, these causes produce effects that cannot be postponed or annulled (they admit no option clause). For the second category (marriage), the author examines subcases: jest in the essence of marriage (e.g., saying 'I marry you' jokingly) renders the jest invalid but the contract binding; jest in the amount of dower leads to different rulings depending on the parties' agreement: if they both disregard the jest (i'tirad), the dower is the higher stated amount (e.g., two thousand), but if they insist on the jest (bina'), the dower is the lower amount (one thousand). This contrasts with sale where a corrupt condition corrupts the contract. For marriage, the dower is subsidiary, so the contract remains valid even if the dower is disputed. The passage further presents disagreements between the Hanafi imams: Abu Hanifa's own position (as narrated by Muhammad or Abu Yusuf) and the position of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). In some cases, when collusion (tawada') occurs (e.g., pretending dower is dinars but actually dirhams), rules of dower of the like apply. For the third category (primary financial element), the ruling follows similar aspects, with ikhtilaf: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, divorce is valid and money is due; according to Abu Hanifa, divorce does not occur (text cuts off before completion). The passage demonstrates how Hanafi law uses hadith and rational argument to derive nuanced rulings, and how internal disagreements are recorded.

Key Terms
الإعراض (al-i'rad): Disregard of the jest, where both parties agree to ignore the jocular intention and adhere to the apparent contract.
البناء (al-bina'): Stipulation – both parties agree to adhere to the jocular amount.
المواضعة (al-muwada'a): Collusion – parties secretly agree on a different term than stated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The higher named amount (2000).
C) Mahr al-mithl.
D) The dower is nullified.

Key Takeaway
In marriage, jest in the essence does not affect validity; jest in dower amount leads to different rules based on parties' subsequent agreement, with a preference for the higher named amount when disregard occurs.

Collusion on Dower Type and Jest in Primary Financial Transactions
Analyzing collusion on dower type and beginning of primary financial transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rules when parties collude on the type of dower (e.g., dinars vs. dirhams) and describe the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two companions regarding jest in primary financial transactions like khul'.

Core Idea
When parties collude on dower type, the named dower prevails if they disregard, but mahr al-mithl applies if they stipulate; in khul', Abu Hanifa differs from Sahibayn on validity.

This lesson covers the third type of jest in marriage: collusion (tawada') on the type of dower (e.g., stating dinars but agreeing on dirhams). It then moves to the third category of transactions where financial element is primary (khul', manumission for money, settlement for homicide), where the ruling becomes more complex and a disagreement emerges between Abu Hanifa and his two companions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the collusion scenario on dower type
Parties pretend to agree on a dower of dinars but secretly intend dirhams. The passage examines what happens if they later agree on i'rad or bina', or differ.
This is more complex than jest in amount because the object is different.
2
Apply the rules: i'rad leads to named type; bina' leads to mahr al-mithl
If they disregard (i'rad), the dower is as named (e.g., dinars). If they stipulate (bina'), the dower of the like (mahr al-mithl) applies by consensus. Contrast with sale where naming is required.
Consensus indicates agreement among Hanafi imams.
3
Note the internal narration differences
When they differ or have no recollection, Muhammad's narration says mahr al-mithl; Abu Yusuf's says the named dower (dinars) is obligatory and the collusion is void. Both differ from the two companions' view (mahr al-mithl).
The 'two' (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) may refer to their own independent view.
4
Move to primary financial transactions
Khul', 'itq 'ala mal, and sulh 'an dam al-'amd: these are acts where money is primary. If parties jest in their essence and agree on stipulation (bina'), Muhammad reports that Sahibayn hold the transaction valid (divorce occurs, money due). Abu Hanifa disagrees: divorce does not occur, equating it to an option (khiyar).
The text cuts off here, but the dispute is clear: Abu Hanifa treats it like a suspended contract.

Effect of Jest in Marriage: The Subsidiary Status of Mahr
Analyzing jest in marriage: subsidiary principle and scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that jest in a subsidiary element (mahr) does not invalidate the marriage contract, and distinguish the outcomes for i'rad, bina', and silence/difference.

Core Idea
In marriage, mahr is subsidiary; jest does not affect validity—the named dower prevails over collusion.

This lesson examines how the Hanafi school treats jest (hazl) and collusive agreements (mawada'at) specifically in the marriage contract, where the dower (mahr) is regarded as a subsidiary element. The passage clarifies that unlike sale—where the price is primary—marriage is valid even if the parties jokingly agree on a false dower. We will explore three scenarios (i'rad, bina', silence/difference) and note a subtle internal difference in Abu Hanifa's narrations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle: mahr as subsidiary
Note the statement that marriage is valid for one thousand because the dower is subsidiary (tabi'). Contrast with sale where the price is primary and jest would invalidate.
2
Analyze the three post-collusion scenarios
For collusion on the dower (e.g., agreeing on dinars but actually dirhams): (a) i'rad (disregarding collusion) → dower as named; (b) bina' (consummation) → mahr al-mithl by consensus; (c) silence or difference → see two narrations from Abu Hanifa.
3
Examine the internal narration difference
According to Muhammad's narration: mahr al-mithl without dispute. According to Abu Yusuf's narration from Abu Hanifa: named dower, voiding collusion. According to the Sahibayn: mahr al-mithl. Note which is considered 'more correct' (asahh).
4
Derive the usul rule
The passage illustrates that the Hanafi school prioritizes the apparent validity of the contract (sihhat al-ijab) over the collusive agreement, especially when the colluded element is subsidiary. This rule can be applied to other subsidiary elements in contracts.

He mentioned from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, that marriage is valid for one thousand, contrary to sale, because the dower is subsidiary in this, so it is not made an object of validity. And Abu Yusuf narrated from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on them both, that the dower is two thousand, for the naming in validity is like the initiation of sale, and in the initiation of sale Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, made acting on the validity of the offer preferable to acting on the validity of the collusive agreement, and likewise this, and this is more correct. And if they collude on dinars, but the dower is in reality dirhams: if they agree on i'rad (disregard), then the dower is according to what they named; if they agree on bina' (building/consummation), then the proper dower (mahr al-mithl) is obligatory, by consensus, contrary to sale because it is not valid except with naming the price, and marriage is valid without naming. And according to them both: divorce occurs and money is obligatory, and the option is void, and likewise this, but it is not limited to three in this, contrary to sale. And if they jest entirely, but they disregard the collusive agreement, divorce occurs and money is obligatory by consensus. And if they differ: then the statement is of the one who claims i'rad according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, because he made that effective in the origin of divorce, and according to them both it is valid and the difference does not benefit. 19 And if they are silent and nothing occurred to them: then it is valid and binding, by consensus. And if they collude on jest in part of the substitute: if they agree on bina': according to them both: divorce occurs and all the money is binding because they made the money binding by way of subsidiary. And according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him: that divorce is tied to her choice because the obligatory divorce is tied to all the substitute, and part of it was tied to the condition. And if they agree on i'rad: divorce becomes binding and all the money. The money And if they agree that nothing occurred to them: divorce occurs and is obligatory according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, because he interpreted that as seriousness and made all that preferable to the collusive agreement, and according to them both likewise because of what we said.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of Hanafi rulings on the effect of jest (hazl) and collusive agreements (mawadaʿat) in contracts, distinguishing between transactions where money is a primary object (e.g., khulʿ, manumission for money) and those where it is secondary (e.g., marriage). The author explores three scenarios: 1) the parties admit collusion and agree on bina' (actual conduct implying intent), 2) they agree on i'rad (disregarding the collusion), 3) they differ or remain silent. The core principle is that in marriage, the dower is subsidiary, so the validity of the contract is not affected by collusion; in khulʿ, money is the primary object, so the opinions diverge. Abu Hanifa analogizes khulʿ to an option condition, delaying divorce until the woman chooses, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that divorce and payment are immediate, voiding the option. The passage also addresses partial jest, where only some of the substitute is subject to collusion. The relied-upon positions are given with reasoning, emphasizing that the school's internal differences arise from whether the contract's object (money) is considered primary or subsidiary, and the presence of an option condition.

Key Terms
الهزل (al-hazl): Jest or joking; a statement made without serious intent that affects the validity of a legal transaction.
المواضعة (al-mawādaʿah): A collusive agreement between parties to misrepresent the actual terms of a contract.
البناء (al-bināʾ): Actual consummation or conduct indicating serious intent after collusion.
الإعراض (al-iʿrāḍ): Disregarding the collusive agreement and treating the outward statement as real.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a marriage where the parties collude on a mahr of 100 dinars but actually intend silver coins, and then they consummate the marriage (bina'), what is the mahr according to the Hanafi school?
A) 100 dinars, because the outward statement stands
B) 100 silver coins, because that was the real intent
C) The proper mahr (mahr al-mithl), by consensus
D) No mahr, because the contract was jest

Key Takeaway
In marriage, the dower is subsidiary, so jest or collusion regarding it does not invalidate the contract; the specific consequences depend on the parties' post-contract behavior (i'rad, bina', silence/difference).

Effect of Jest in Khul': Internal Hanafi Dispute on Khiyar al-Shart
Analyzing jest in money-primary contracts: internal dispute on khiyar analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding jest in khul', explain the analogy to khiyar al-shart, and apply the rulings to scenarios of full and partial jest.

Core Idea
In khul', money is primary; Abu Hanifa suspends divorce on woman's choice (khiyar), while Sahibayn enforce divorce and payment immediately.

This lesson shifts from marriage to contracts where money is the primary object, such as khul' (divorce for compensation), manumission for money, and compromise for intentional homicide. Here the Hanafi school faces a clear internal split: Abu Hanifa analogizes the jest scenario to an option condition (khiyar al-shart), suspending the divorce until the woman chooses, while his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the divorce is immediate and the compensation binding. The passage also covers partial jest, i'rad, and silence, providing a comprehensive view of how the school treats intent in these financial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category: contracts where money is primary
These include khul', manumission for money, and compromise for homicide. Unlike marriage, money is the main object, so jest in the origin of the contract may have greater effect. Contrast with the previous lesson on marriage.
2
Analyze full jest with bina' in khul'
If the parties jokingly agree on khul' but then both admit they acted (bina'), the Sahibayn say divorce occurs and money is binding immediately. Abu Hanifa says divorce does not occur until the woman chooses, because he analogizes to khiyar al-shart (option condition) where the woman has the right to decide.
The passage notes that this is not limited to three (unlike sale) but shares the essence.
3
Examine other scenarios: i'rad, difference, silence
If they jest but then disregard (i'rad), divorce and money are due by consensus. If they differ (one claims i'rad, the other silence), Abu Hanifa says the one claiming i'rad is believed (since it affects the origin of divorce); the Sahibayn say the difference does not matter (contract is valid). If they remain silent, it is also valid and binding by consensus.
4
Apply to partial jest
If the jest only involves part of the compensation (e.g., they collude on 500 of 1000), the Sahibayn hold that all money is binding (by way of subsidiary). Abu Hanifa says divorce is tied to the woman's choice because the full compensation is conditionally bound.
5
Derive the usul rule: khiyar al-shart analogy in khul'
Abu Hanifa's position is based on treating the collusive jest as an implied condition of choice. The Sahibayn reject this because they see jest as void, thus the contract is fully effective. This difference illustrates how analogical reasoning (qiyas) is applied differently within the school.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of Hanafi rulings on the effect of jest (hazl) and collusive agreements (mawadaʿat) in contracts, distinguishing between transactions where money is a primary object (e.g., khulʿ, manumission for money) and those where it is secondary (e.g., marriage). The author explores three scenarios: 1) the parties admit collusion and agree on bina' (actual conduct implying intent), 2) they agree on i'rad (disregarding the collusion), 3) they differ or remain silent. The core principle is that in marriage, the dower is subsidiary, so the validity of the contract is not affected by collusion; in khulʿ, money is the primary object, so the opinions diverge. Abu Hanifa analogizes khulʿ to an option condition, delaying divorce until the woman chooses, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that divorce and payment are immediate, voiding the option. The passage also addresses partial jest, where only some of the substitute is subject to collusion. The relied-upon positions are given with reasoning, emphasizing that the school's internal differences arise from whether the contract's object (money) is considered primary or subsidiary, and the presence of an option condition.

Key Terms
الخلع (al-khulʿ): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation paid to the husband.
خيار الشرط (khiyār al-sharṭ): Option condition where a party reserves the right to cancel a contract within a specified period.
العتق على مال (al-ʿitq ʿalā māl): Manumission of a slave in exchange for payment.
الصلح عن دم العمد (al-ṣulḥ ʿan dam al-ʿamd): Compromise in intentional homicide, where the victim's family accepts monetary compensation instead of retaliation.
البدل (al-badal): The substitute or compensation in a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In money-primary contracts like khul', jest in the origin with subsequent consummation leads to immediate divorce and payment according to the Sahibayn (the mufta bihi); Abu Hanifa suspends the divorce on the woman's choice by analogy to khiyar al-shart.

The Legal Effect of Mockingly Declaring Islam: An Usul Analysis
Ruling on mock declaration of Islam with analogy to duress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul concept of insha' to determine that mock declarations of faith are legally effective, and identify the analogical reasoning (qiyas) used to derive this ruling.

Core Idea
A disbeliever who mockingly utters the shahada becomes Muslim — the declaration (insha') takes effect instantly, like duress.

This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi addresses a critical boundary: the legal effect of a disbeliever's mock declaration of Islam. At first glance, jest might seem to negate seriousness, but Hanafi usul holds that the utterance of the shahada is an act of insha' (declaration) that cannot be rescinded. The text draws an analogy (qiyas) to the case of one under duress (mukrah), whose coerced declaration is also valid. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the nature of insha' and how analogical reasoning extends the ruling from duress to mockery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a disbeliever joking with the word of Islam (hazl bi kalimat al-Islam) and renouncing his religion mockingly. The ruling is that he is judged to have entered Islam (yajibu an yuhkama bi-imanihi).
2
Locate the analogy (qiyas)
The text equates this case to the case of one under duress (mukrah) whose forced declaration is also effective. The illa (common cause) is that both are declarations (insha') that take effect immediately and cannot be rejected or delayed.
The passage says 'like the one under duress'.
3
Identify the usul principle
The passage states 'because it is in the status of a declaration (insha') whose ruling does not accept rejection or delay.' This reveals that the category of insha' carries legal finality.
The shahada is an insha' and therefore its effect is instantaneous and irrevocable.
4
Apply the qiyas
The asl (original case) is the coerced declaration (mukrah), the far' (new case) is the mock declaration, the illa is the nature of insha' (immediate effect, no possibility of rejection), and the hukm is the same: the declaration is valid and Islam is confirmed.
5
Note the implication for intention
The passage implies that intention (niyyah) is not a condition for the validity of the declaration in this context. This is a key distinction from many other acts where intention matters.
The Hanafi position on mockery is strict: even jest does not negate the legal effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما الكافر إذا هزل بكلمة الإسلام، وتبرأ عن دينه هازلاً يجب أن يُحكم بإيمانه، كالمكره؛ لأنه بمنزلة إنشاء لا يحتمل حكمه الردَّ والتراخي. والله أعلم.
As for the disbeliever if he jokes with the word of Islam and renounces his religion jokingly, it is necessary to judge him as a believer, like the one under duress; because it is in the status of a declaration (insha') whose ruling does not accept rejection or delay. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 769

Commentary
This passage states a ruling from Hanafi fiqh: if a disbeliever mockingly utters the declaration of Islam or renounces his religion as a joke, he is considered to have entered Islam immediately. The reasoning is that the utterance of the shahada is an act of insha' (declaration), which takes effect instantly. Unlike contracts that can be rescinded, this declaration cannot be rejected or delayed. The analogy (qiyas) is to the case of one under duress (mukrah) whose coerced declaration of Islam is also valid. This ruling emphasizes the seriousness of the words of Islam, even when said in jest.

Key Terms
إنشاء (insha'): A declarative utterance that brings about a legal effect immediately, such as pronouncing the shahada.
مكره (mukrah): One who is coerced into an action; in this context, a person forced to declare Islam is still considered a Muslim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The analogy to duress proves that coercion cancels the need for sincerity.
C) The text says the shahada is like a contract that cannot be revoked.
D) The ruling depends on whether the disbeliever was serious or not.

Key Takeaway
A disbeliever who mockingly declares Islam is ruled a Muslim because the shahada is an insha' that takes effect instantly, analogous to a declaration under duress.

Safah (Foolishness) and Its Legal Effect on Capacity
Defines safah and discusses its effect on legal capacity and wealth restriction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define safah (foolishness) as a defect in legal capacity, identify its effect on wealth restriction, and explain the Hanafi position on the requirement of sound judgment (rushd).

Core Idea
Safah restricts wealth until sound judgment (rushd) is evident; it is wastefulness contrary to Shari'ah and reason.

This lesson examines the concept of safah (foolishness/extravagance) as a defect in legal capacity (ahliyyah) according to Hanafi usul al-fiqh. Understanding safah is crucial because it limits a person's financial independence and requires the restoration of sound judgment (rushd). The Quran provides the primary evidence, and there is a key difference among scholars regarding the appointment of a guardian.

Guided Path
1
1. Define safah and its components
Extract the definition from the passage: acting contrary to Shari'ah in some respect, following desire, and contrary to intellect, even if the origin is permissible. It includes wastefulness (israf) and extravagance (tabdhir).
Note that safah does not require the act to be inherently impermissible.
2
2. Identify the legal effect of safah on capacity
Safah is a defect in legal capacity (ahliyyah). It does not prevent any rulings of Shari'ah (e.g., prayer, fasting) but restricts the safih from independently managing wealth.
3
3. Examine the Quranic evidence for wealth restriction
The Quran (4:5-6) commands not to give wealth to the foolish and conditions giving upon sensing sound judgment (rushd). This is the primary dalil.
The passage notes consensus (ijma) on this restriction.
4
4. Understand the condition of rushd and its indefinite nature
Abu Hanifa explains that at puberty, safah may persist. With time and experience, rushd necessarily occurs. The indefinite noun 'rushd' implies any degree of sound judgment removes the restriction.
If doubt enters or the condition is deemed existent, the restriction lifts.
5
5. Evaluate the dispute over appointing a guardian
The passage begins an ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa holds that safah (wilful obstinacy) does not justify mandatory guardianship, whereas others argue for it. This will be explored further.
The full dispute is not concluded in this passage but sets the stage.

Source Text

Safah (foolishness). The fourth section: It is safah. It is: acting contrary to the requirement of the Shariah in some respect, following desire, and contrary to the indication of intellect even if its origin is permissible. And it is wastefulness and extravagance, because the origin of sale, righteousness, charity, and donations is permissible, but wastefulness is forbidden, like wastefulness in food and drink. And that is a defect in [legal] capacity, and it does not prevent anything of the rulings of Shariah, nor does it necessitate placing the address [of the law] aside at all. And they agreed that he is prevented from his wealth at the beginning of his reaching puberty by the text. Allah Almighty said: 'And do not give the foolish your wealth' [An-Nisa 4:5]. Then He conditioned giving upon sensing sound judgment from them, saying: 'Then if you sense from them sound judgment, then give them their wealth' [An-Nisa 4:6]. Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said: At the onset of puberty, safah may not leave him. Then if time extends and experience and testing appear, a kind of sound judgment necessarily occurs. And the condition is sound judgment (rushd) as an indefinite noun, so the prohibition falls away. Because it is either a punishment or a ruling whose meaning is not understood and thus attaches to the specific text. So if doubt enters it, or the condition becomes in the status of existence in some way, its consequence becomes necessary. And they differed regarding the obligation of appointing a guardian for the safih: Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Since safah is obstinacy and abandonment of what is obligatory knowingly, it cannot be a cause for guardianship. Do you not see that one who...
Source pages p. 770

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul work defines safah, which is a defect in legal capacity (ahliyyah) involving foolishness and extravagance. The definition emphasizes that safah involves acting against Shari'ah and reason, even if the base act (like trade or charity) is permissible. Wastefulness is prohibited. Safah does not invalidate any rulings of Shari'ah but is a restriction on financial transactions. There is consensus (ijma) based on Quran that the safih is prevented from his wealth upon reaching puberty until sound judgment (rushd) is evident. Abu Hanifa clarifies that at the onset of puberty, safah may persist, but with time and experience, rushd necessarily develops. The condition of rushd is indefinite, so if there is any doubt or if it is deemed existent, the restriction lifts. The passage then begins a dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding the obligation of appointing a guardian (nazar) for the safih. Abu Hanifa argues that safah, being a willful obstinacy, cannot justify guardianship.

Key Terms
سفه (safah): Foolishness, specifically acting contrary to Shari'ah and reason through wastefulness and extravagance, affecting legal capacity.
رشد (rushd): Sound judgment, the condition that, when present, removes the restriction on the safih's wealth.
أهلية (ahliyyah): Legal capacity; safah is a defect in capacity that restricts financial transactions.
مكابرة (mukaabarah): Obstinacy, a characteristic of safah that Abu Hanifa uses to argue against mandatory guardianship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When he reaches age 18.
B) When he repents.
C) When sound judgment (rushd) is sensed.
D) When a guardian consents.

Key Takeaway
Safah is a defect in legal capacity that restricts wealth until sound judgment (rushd) is demonstrated, based on Quranic evidence and Hanafi usul.

Safah (Foolishness) and the Obligation of Guardianship
Defining safah and discussing the obligation of guardianship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define safah, explain its effect on legal capacity, and identify the Hanafi dispute on the obligation of guardianship (nazar).

Core Idea
Safah is a defect in legal capacity that restricts property disposal but does not lift the divine address.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul clarifies the legal concept of safah (foolishness) and its consequences. While safah leads to a temporary restriction on managing wealth, it does not nullify the safih's capacity for other actions like contracts, divorces, or criminal liability. A central intra-madhhab debate arises: is it obligatory to appoint a guardian over the safih? Abu Hanifa says it is permissible but not obligatory; the Sahibayn argue for obligation.

Guided Path
1
1. Define safah and identify its legal elements
Read the definition: 'acting contrary to the requirement of the Law from one aspect, following whim, and contrary to rational indication, even if the act's origin is lawful.' Note that safah is extravagance and wastefulness – it is a defect in ahliyyah (legal capacity) but does not remove the person from being a mukallaf.
Safah is not kufr or insanity; it preserves the essential capacity.
2
2. Examine the Quranic evidence for restriction
The passage cites Q.4:5-6. The first verse forbids giving wealth to the foolish; the second conditions return of wealth on perceived rushd (sound judgment). Notice that the restriction is temporary and conditional.
The Quranic text is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalala – the precise application to safah requires ijtihad.
3
3. Understand Abu Hanifa's position on guardianship
Abu Hanifa argues that guardianship (nazar) is permissible (ja'iz) but not obligatory. Safah is obstinacy, so it cannot be a cause for guardianship. Making it obligatory would suspend the safih's legal capacity, causing greater harm than leaving him unrestricted.
He analogizes to the major sinner who remains addressed by the Law despite negligence.
4
4. Understand the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that guardianship is obligatory (wajib) as a right of the Muslims and for the safih's own religious benefit. They argue that preventing harm (e.g., to neighbors) justifies restriction.
They draw an analogy to preventing a property owner from harming neighbors – a known ruling from Abu Yusuf.
5
5. Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
According to Abu Hanifa, the safih's statements (e.g., sales, divorces) remain valid even if he is wasteful; a guardian may be appointed optionally. For the Sahibayn, guardianship is obligatory, and the guardian can invalidate harmful transactions.
The dispute affects whether the safih's contracts are binding or subject to annulment.

Source Text

القسم الرابع وهو السفه هو : العمل بخلاف موجب الشرع من وجه، واتباع الهوى، وخلاف دلالة العقل وإن كان أصله مشروعاً. وهو الإسراف والتبذير ؛ لأن أصل البيع والبر والإحسان والتبرعات مشروع، إلا أن الإسراف حرام، كالإسراف من الطعام والشراب، وذلك خلَلاً في الأهلية، ولا يمنع شيئاً من أحكام الشرع، ولا يوجب وضع الخطاب بحال. وأجمعوا على أنه يُمنع عنه ماله في أول ما يبلغ بالنص، قال الله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَا تُؤْتُوا السُّفَهَاءَ أَمْوَلَكُمْ ﴾ النساء / ٥ ، ثم علق الإيتاء بإيناس من الرشد، فقال: ﴿فَإِنْ وَانَسْتُم مِنْهُمْ رُشْدًا فَادْفَعُوا إِلَيْهِمْ أَمْوَهُمْ ﴾ النساء / ٦. قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : أول أحوال البلوغ قد لا يُفارقه السفه، فإذا امتد الزمان، وظهرت الخبرة والتجربة حدث ضرب من الرشد لا محالة. والشرط رشد نكرة، فسقط المنع ؛ لأنه إما عقوبة وإما حكم لا يُعقل معناه فيتعلق بعين النص، فإذا دخله شبهة، أو صار الشرط في حكم الوجود بوجه وجب جزاؤه. واختلفوا في وجوب النظر للسفيه : فقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : لما كان السفه مكابرة، وتركاً لما الواجب عن علم ومعرفة : لم يجز أن يكون سبباً للنظر، ألا يُرى أن من قصر في حقوق الله عزَّ وجلَّ مَجانةً وسفهاً : لم يوضع عنه الخطاب نظراً، بل كان مؤكّداً لازماً، وقد يُحبس عقوبةً ولا يوضع عنه الخطاب، ولا تبطل في ذلك عباراته ، ولا تُعطّل عليه أسباب الحدود والعقوبات. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله : النظر واجب؛ حقاً للمسلمين، وحقاً له لدينه، لا السفهه ألا يرى أن العفو عن صاحب الكبيرة حسن في الدنيا والآخرة وإن أصرَّ عليها، وقاساه بمنع المال. وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : النظر من هذا الوجه جائز، لا واجب، كما في صاحب الكبيرة، وإنما يَحسُنُ إذا لم يتضمن ضرراً فوقه، وها هنا يتضمن ضرراً فوقه وهو وقف أهليته وإلحاقه بالصبيان والمجانين والبهائم. بخلاف منع المال ؛ لما قلنا إنه غيرُ معقول، ولأنه عقوبة لا تحتمل المقايسة، ولأن اليدَ للآدمي نعمةٌ زائدة واللسانَ والأهلية نعمةٌ أصلية. فبطل القياس لإبطال أعلى النّعمتين ؛ باعتبار أدناهما. وقالا : هذه الأمور صارت حقاً للعبد ؛ رفقاً به، فإذا أدى إلى الضرر : وجب الردُّ لدفع الضرر عن المسلمين وإن لم يكن للمسلمين حق في عين المال. وهذا قياس ما روي عن أبي يوسف رحمه الله فيمن تصرف في خالص ملکه بما يضرُّ جيرانه : أنه يُمنع عنه، فصار الحجر عندهما مشروعاً بطريق النظر، وإنما يجب أن يُنظر إلى ما فيه نظر له أبداً، فلا يلحق بالصبي خاصةً ولا بالمريض، ولا بالمكره، لكن يجب إثبات النظر بأي أصل أمكن اعتباره، على ما هو مذكور في المبسوط»
The fourth section: Safah (foolishness). It is: acting contrary to the requirement of the Law from one aspect, following whim, and contrary to the indication of reason, even if its origin is lawful. It is extravagance and wastefulness; because the origin of sale, piety, charity, and donations is lawful, except that extravagance is forbidden, like extravagance in food and drink. That is a defect in legal capacity, yet it does not prevent any of the rulings of the Law, nor does it necessitate the lifting of the address at all. And they are agreed that he is prevented from his wealth at the beginning of his attaining puberty by the text. Allah Almighty said: 'And do not give the foolish your wealth' [an-Nisa 4:5], then He made the giving conditional upon sensing sound judgment in them, saying: 'And if you perceive in them sound judgment, then give over to them their wealth' [an-Nisa 4:6]. Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, said: At the beginning of puberty, safah may not be separated from him. When time extends and experience and trial appear, a kind of sound judgment occurs inevitably. The condition is sound judgment in an indefinite form, so the prohibition falls away; because it is either a punishment or a ruling whose meaning is not intellectually conceivable, thus it attaches to the exact text. When it becomes ambiguous, or the condition is in effect existent in some way, its consequence becomes obligatory. And they differed concerning the obligation of guardianship for the safih. Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Since safah is obstinacy and abandonment of what is obligatory with knowledge and understanding, it cannot be a cause for guardianship. Do you not see that one who is remiss in the rights of Allah Almighty out of jest and foolishness is not relieved of the address as a matter of guardianship; rather, it is confirmed and binding. He may be imprisoned as a punishment but the address is not removed from him, nor are his statements nullified in that regard, nor are the causes of prescribed punishments and penalties suspended for him. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, said: Guardianship is obligatory as a right of the Muslims and a right for him regarding his religion, not because of the safah. Do you not see that forgiveness of the committer of a major sin is good in this world and the Hereafter even if he persists in it, and they analogized him to the prevention of wealth. And Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Guardianship from this aspect is permissible, not obligatory, just as in the case of the committer of a major sin. It is only good if it does not entail a greater harm, and here it entails a greater harm, namely suspending his legal capacity and equating him with children, insane persons, and animals. Unlike the prevention of wealth, because we said it is not intellectually conceivable, and because it is a punishment that does not admit analogy, and because the hand of a human is an additional blessing while the tongue and legal capacity are an original blessing. So the analogy fails because it would invalidate the higher of the two blessings by considering the lower. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: These matters became a right of the servant as a kindness to him; so if they lead to harm, prevention becomes obligatory to repel harm from the Muslims even if the Muslims have no right to the exact wealth. And this is analogous to what was narrated from Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, regarding one who disposes of his exclusive property in a way that harms his neighbors: that he is prevented from it. Thus, legal interdiction according to them becomes legislated by way of guardianship, and it is necessary to consider what is always in his best interest. So he is not equated specifically to a child, nor to a sick person, nor to one under compulsion; rather, guardianship must be established by whatever principle possible, as is mentioned in al-Mabsut.
Source pages p. 770

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul discusses the legal concept of safah (foolishness/prodigality) and its effect on legal capacity. It begins by defining safah as acting contrary to the divine law from one aspect, following whims, and opposing rational indications, even if the act's origin is lawful. Safah manifests as extravagance and wastefulness; although actions like selling, giving charity, and donations are fundamentally permitted, excessive indulgence becomes prohibited. This defect (khalal) in legal capacity (ahliyyah) does not invalidate any other rulings of Shariah nor does it lift the divine address from the person. The passage then cites Quranic evidence (4:5-6) for preventing the safih from managing his wealth at the onset of puberty. According to Abu Hanifa, at puberty safah may still be present; only after time and experience does sound judgment (rushd) emerge. The condition of rushd is indefinite, so the restriction falls away once rushd is perceived. The core dispute in this passage is whether it is obligatory (wajib) to appoint a guardian (nazar) over the safih. Abu Hanifa argues it is not obligatory; safah is obstinacy and deliberate neglect, so it should not be a cause for guardianship. He draws an analogy: one who neglects Allah's rights out of jest is still addressed by the Law and punished, not relieved of obligations. Making guardianship obligatory would equate the safih with children and the insane, causing greater harm. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that guardianship is obligatory as a right of the community and for the safih's own religious benefit. They compare it to forbidding a major sinner from sin (which is advised but not legally enforced) and to the prevention of wealth (hajr). Abu Hanifa responds that such guardianship is permissible (ja'iz) but not obligatory, and in this case it entails excessive harm (suspending legal capacity). The passage concludes with a reference to al-Mabsut for further details.

Key Terms
السفه (safah): Acting contrary to the requirement of the Law from one aspect, following whim, and contrary to rational indication, even if the act's origin is lawful. It is extravagance and wastefulness.
النظر (nazar): Guardianship or supervision over a person's affairs; here specifically appointing a guardian to manage the safih's property.
الرشد (rushd): Sound judgment and maturity in managing wealth; the condition for lifting the restriction on the safih.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the safih?
A) Whether the safih should be prevented from all legal actions.
B) Whether it is obligatory (wajib) to appoint a guardian over the safih.
C) Whether the safih can be held responsible for criminal acts.
D) Whether Quranic verses on safah are abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Safah is a defect in legal capacity that restricts property disposal but does not remove the divine address or nullify other legal acts; the obligation of guardianship is a point of ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
